Let $n \ge 3$ be a given integer. Determine all nonnegative integers $k$ such that there exist $n$ distinct lines in the plane satisfying both of the following:
\begin{itemize}
    \item for all positive integers $a$ and $b$ with $a+b\le n+1$, the point $(a,b)$ lies on at least one of the lines; and
    \item exactly $k$ of the $n$ lines are sunny.

- For all positive integers a and b with a+b <= n+1, the point (a,b) lies on at least one of the lines.
- Exactly k of the n lines are sunny.

Definition: A line is sunny if it is not parallel to any of the x-axis, the y-axis, or the line x+y=0. So sunny lines are those that are not horizontal, not vertical, and not with slope -1 (since x+y=0 => y = -x, slope -1). Actually "parallel to the line x+y=0" means lines of slope -1, because they are parallel to that line. So sunny lines are those that are not horizontal, not vertical, and not slope -1. So sunny lines are lines with slope ≠ 0, ≠ ∞, ≠ -1.

So we need to characterize feasible k.

We should approach systematically: given n lines (distinct). Let L be the set of lines. For each point P (a,b) with a,b>=1, a+b <= n+1, there exists a line L in L such that P lies on L. We want to know possible numbers of sunny lines among L.

Let's analyze coverage requirement with lines. Suppose we have n lines. Consider the points with a+b = n+1, which are on the "hypotenuse". There are n points: (1,n),(2,n-1),...,(n,1). Let's denote non-sunny lines = lines that are either horizontal (slope 0), vertical (slope ∞), or slope -1. They are not sunny. They can be used to cover points.

Actually a sunny line could pass through multiple points on that line if they are collinear. But those points are on the line x+y = n+1, which is a line with slope -1. Sunny lines are those not parallel to x+y=0, so they are not slope -1. Therefore, a sunny line cannot be parallel to x+y = n+1. However, it could intersect that line at a single point. So any sunny line can intersect the line x+y = n+1 at most one point (since two distinct points on that line would determine the line, and if a sunny line passes through two points of that line, then it would be coincident with that line (since two points determine a line), but that line is x+y = n+1 (or a line with slope -1). But sunny lines are not parallel to x+y=0, but they could be coincident with x+y = n+1? That line is parallel to x+y=0, so it has slope -1. Therefore, it is not sunny. So a sunny line cannot contain two points from the line x+y = n+1 because that would make it equal to that line (since two points determine a line) and thus have slope -1, contradicting sunniness. So a sunny line can cover at most one point from the set of points with a+b = n+1. (Similarly, it can cover points with a+b < n+1, but we are focusing on the hypotenuse.)

We need to solve the problem: "Determine all nonnegative integers k such that there exist n distinct lines in the plane satisfying both of the following: for all positive integers a and b with a+b ≤ n+1, the point (a,b) lies on at least one of the lines; and exactly k of the n lines are sunny." Let's analyze more systematically.

Let n ≥ 3 be given. Let P_n = {(a,b) ∈ ℕ^2 : a,b ≥ 1, a+b ≤ n+1}. We have |P_n| = n(n+1)/2.

We have n lines L_1,...,L_n distinct. Let S be the set of sunny lines among them. |S| = k.

We need to cover P_n. The lines that are not sunny (i.e., not sunny) are those that are parallel to x-axis, y-axis, or x+y=0. They are non-sunny. Let's denote N = n - k (non-sunny lines). They are of types: horizontal (H), vertical (V), slope -1 (D). (We could also have lines that are parallel to these but not exactly those, but they are all lines with those slopes.)

We need to find all k for which there exists a covering of P_n by n lines with exactly k sunny lines. That's the problem.

We have 15 points. We want to maximize coverage with 3 lines. The maximum possible union size from 3 non-sunny lines is something like 15? But we can't reach 15 because of overlaps. Let's compute union size for some triples. The triple we considered (x=1,y=1,x+y=6) gave 12. Is there a triple that gives 13 or 14? Let's try combinations.

- Use two horizontal lines (y=1 and y=2) and one vertical (x=1). Compute union.

H1: y=1 covers 5 points (a=1..5). H2: y=2 covers 4 points (a=1..4). V1: x=1 covers 5 points (b=1..5). Overlaps: H1 and V1 intersect at (1,1). H2 and V1 intersect at (1,2). H1 and H2 are disjoint (different y). So union size = |H1| + |H2| + |V1| - overlaps (but careful: overlaps between H1 and V1, H2 and V1). There is no overlap between H1 and H2. So union = 5 + 4 + 5 - 2 (since (1,1) and (1,2) are common) = 12. So still 12.

- Use two vertical lines (x=1 and x=2) and one horizontal (y=1). Similar: V1 (5), V2 (4), H1 (5) => union = 5+4+5 - (overlap V1&H1: (1,1); V2&H1: (2,1)) = 5+4+5-2=12.

- Use one horizontal (y=1), one vertical (x=1), and one slope -1 (x+y=5). That's D5. D5 covers 4 points (a=1..4, b=4..1). Overlaps: with H1 (y=1) overlap at (5,1)? Wait D5 covers (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1). Overlap with H1 (y=1): (4,1) is common. Overlap with V1 (x=1): (1,4) is common. So union = 5+5+4 - 2 (overlaps) = 12. That's correct. So any triple that includes these three lines will have union 12. If we use a different combination, maybe we can get 13 if overlaps are fewer. For example, if we use two lines that are disjoint and both cover 5 points, but they can't be disjoint because they intersect. For example, take H1 (5), D6 (5), and some other line that covers 4 points but intersects them in fewer points? But we want to maximize union, so we want to cover as many distinct points as possible. The total points are 15. The sum of sizes of three lines is at most 15, but if they overlap a lot, union is less. So to get union 13 or 14, we need the overlaps to be very small, like at most 2. But can we find three lines (each H,V,D) such that the sum of sizes is at least 13 and overlaps are ≤2? The maximum sum of sizes is 5+5+5 = 15, but overlaps are at least 3 because any two lines intersect? Do any two lines of these types always intersect? Not necessarily. For example, a horizontal line and a vertical line intersect at one point. A horizontal line and a slope -1 line intersect at one point. A vertical line and a slope -1 line intersect at one point. So any two distinct lines among these three types (if they are of different types) intersect in exactly one point. However, if they are of the same type (e.g., two horizontal lines), they are parallel and never intersect. But they are distinct, so they are parallel. So two horizontal lines are parallel, so they have no intersection. Similarly, two vertical lines are parallel. Two slope -1 lines are parallel (since they have slope -1). So depending on the combination, we could have fewer overlaps.

But to achieve a large union, we might want to use lines that are all of the same type? For example, three horizontal lines (y=1,y=2,y=3) cover 5+4+3 = 12 points, no overlaps (since parallel). That's 12 points. Similarly, three vertical lines: x=1,x=2,x=3 => 5+4+3 = 12. Three slope -1 lines: x+y=2,3,4 => 1+2+3 = 6; or x+y=3,4,5 => 2+3+4 = 9; or x+y=4,5,6 => 3+4+5 = 12. So using three slope -1 lines, we can cover up to 12 points (with sums 4,5,6). That's also 12. So indeed, the maximum union from three non-sunny lines appears to be 12, but we need to confirm if there is a triple that yields 13. Suppose we take two lines of types that intersect (like H1 and V1) covering 5+5-1=9, then add a third line that is, say, D6 covering 5 points. Overlaps with H1 and V1 each are 1, but those overlap points may be distinct, so total union = 9+5 - (overlap between D6 and H1) - (overlap between D6 and V1) + (overlap between H1 and V1 already accounted). But we need to compute properly: Union size = |A∪B∪C| = |A|+|B|+|C| - |A∩B| - |A∩C| - |B∩C| + |A∩B∩C|. For A=H1, B=V1, C=D6. |A∩B| = 1, |A∩C| = 1, |B∩C| = 1. |A∩B∩C|: is there a point common to all three? That would be a point with a=1, b=1, and also a+b=6 => 2=6 impossible. So 0. So union = 5+5+5 - 1 - 1 - 1 = 12. That's correct.

What if we choose two lines that are parallel (e.g., H1 and H2) covering 5+4 = 9, and then add a third line that is, say, V1 covering 5 points. Overlaps: H1∩V1=1, H2∩V1=1, H1∩H2=0. So union = 5+4+5 - 1 - 1 = 12. That's also 12. So indeed, any triple that includes at least one line of each type or parallels seems to have union 12. Could we get 13 if we pick lines that cover 5 points but with only 2 overlaps? For example, take H1 (5), V1 (5), and D5 (4). Overlaps: H1∩V1=1, H1∩D5=1, V1∩D5=1? Check V1 (x=1) and D5 (x+y=5): (1,4) is common => 1. So union = 5+5+4 - 3 = 11. So less. So indeed 12 seems max.

Thus, with 3 non-sunny lines, the maximum number of points they can cover is 12. That means at least 3 points are uncovered. Then we have 2 sunny lines left. As argued, those 3 uncovered points (if they are of the type where any pair determines a non-sunny line) cannot be covered by 2 sunny lines. However, is it possible that the uncovered points after some triple of non-sunny lines are not all in that "bad" set? The uncovered points are the complement of the union. The union size is at most 12, so uncovered points are at least 3. The worst-case (most difficult for sunny lines) would be if the uncovered points form a set where each pair determines a non-sunny line. But we can bound it by something like the maximum possible number of points on a line in the triangle. The triangle is of size n+1, so the maximum number of collinear lattice points is at most n+1? But many lines have few points. However, to prove an upper bound on k, we could argue that each sunny line can cover at most ⌊(n+1)/2⌋ points (maybe). But is that always true? Consider a line with slope 1/2 (y = (1/2)x + c). That's sunny. The points on that line are integer lattice points when x is even. For example, line y = (1/2)x. It passes through (2,1), (4,2), (6,3), ... So number of points within triangle is about (n+1)/4. That's less than n/2. So indeed, the maximum is achieved by lines with slope 1 (or maybe slope 0? Not sunny). So the maximum points a sunny line can cover is floor((n+1)/2). But is that always achievable? For n=3, floor(4/2)=2. That's the line y=x (which is sunny). That's 2 points. For n=4, floor(5/2)=2. That's line y=x, covering (1,1),(2,2) → 2 points. That's less than 3. So indeed, the maximum number of points on a sunny line is ⌊(n+1)/2⌋. But we need to consider that a sunny line could be chosen not to pass through the origin, but still with slope 1, like y = x + c. For c > 0, the line passes through points with a+b = 2a + c. So indeed, the line y=x (c=0) gives the maximum, with ⌊(n+1)/2⌋ points. So maximum points on a sunny line is ⌊(n+1)/2⌋. That's roughly n/2.

Now, if we have m non-sunny lines, they cover at most something like (some bound). But we don't have a bound on how many points a non-sunny line can cover. For example, a horizontal line y=1 covers ⌊n/1? Actually y=1 covers points with b=1, a ≤ n, so n points (since a+b≤n+1 => a≤n). So a non-sunny line can cover up to n points. Similarly, a vertical line x=1 covers n points. A slope -1 line with sum n+1 covers n points. So non-sunny lines are very efficient. So to minimize the number of non-sunny lines (i.e., maximize sunny lines), we would like to use as many non-sunny lines as necessary to cover the points. But we have a fixed total number of lines n. So if we want many sunny lines, we need to use few non-sunny lines. That's the trade-off. So we need to find the minimum possible number of non-sunny lines needed to cover P_n (with the help of sunny lines). But we are allowed to have sunny lines that also cover points. Let's attempt a rigorous proof. We'll denote N_n = n(n+1)/2. Let L be the set of lines. Let S be sunny lines, |S| = k. Let N = n - k be non-sunny lines. We'll try to bound k.

Observation 1: Each non-sunny line can cover many points, but sunny lines are limited. So that doesn't force a bound on k. So that approach doesn't give an upper bound.

Observation 2: The triangle P_n contains points with both coordinates large. The points with a,b ≥ 2 and a+b ≤ n+1 are many. The lines that are not sunny are of three types. For n=5, that's 4*3/2=6 points. So the set of points with a,b ≥ 2 is a smaller triangle of size (n-2) (if we shift). So that's a triangle of lattice points with coordinates ≥2, a+b ≤ n+1. Now, consider covering these interior points. A sunny line cannot be horizontal or vertical or slope -1. Actually let's compute for a sunny line with slope 1 (y=x), it passes through points where a=b. For a,b ≥2, a=b can be up to floor((n+1)/2). That's about n/2. So that's a decent number. So that doesn't restrict.

Maybe we need to use a different combinatorial argument: Count the number of lines that are not sunny, and relate it to the covering requirement via an inequality involving the sum of sizes of intersections or something. Let's think about the fact that each line is either sunny or not. If we have many sunny lines, then there are few non-sunny lines. The non-sunny lines are limited in number. But perhaps we can prove that each non-sunny line can cover at most something like (n) points, and that the total number of points covered by the non-sunny lines is at most something, and then the sunny lines must cover the rest. But we don't have a bound on how many points a sunny line can cover; it could be up to ⌊(n+1)/2⌋. So if we have m non-sunny lines, they cover at most m * n points (if each covers n points). But they could overlap. So the maximum distinct points they can cover is at most something like m * n, but we need to cover N_n points. So if m is small, then the sunny lines must cover the rest, and the maximum capacity of sunny lines is (n - m) * ⌊(n+1)/2⌋. So we can derive an inequality: (n - m) * ⌊(n+1)/2⌋ ≥ N_n - (something). But that doesn't guarantee impossibility; we need to show that if k > ⌊(n-1)/2⌋ then such inequality fails. But note that m = n - k. So we are assuming k is large, m is small. Then (n - m) = k. So the capacity of sunny lines is k * ⌊(n+1)/2⌋. The non-sunny lines can cover up to m * n points, but they may not be able to cover all points because of overlaps. But we can get an upper bound on how many points can be covered by m non-sunny lines (distinct lines) and the rest must be covered by sunny lines. However, we don't have a guarantee that the sunny lines can cover any arbitrary set; they have to be placed such that they cover the points not covered by non-sunny lines. But we can choose the lines. So the question is: for a given m (small), is there a configuration of m non-sunny lines and (n - m) sunny lines that together cover all P_n? We can try to construct such a configuration, but we want to prove an upper bound on k. That is, we want to show that if k is too large, then any covering must have at least some number of non-sunny lines, i.e., m ≥ something. That's a necessary condition. So we need to prove: In any covering of P_n by n lines, the number of non-sunny lines is at least ⌈n/2⌉? But that's false for n=4 (we have m≥3). So it's not that simple. So we need a more refined bound. Let's compute for n=5, we suspect maximum k is 2, so m ≥ 3. That is m ≥ 3. For n=3, m ≥ 2. So m ≥ ⌈n/2⌉? For n=3, ⌈3/2⌉=2, okay. For n=4, ⌈4/2⌉=2, but we need m ≥ 3, so that's not sufficient. So we need to show that m ≥ ⌊(n+2)/2⌋? Let's compute: For n=4, (n+2)/2 = 3, floor =3. For n=3, (5)/2=2.5, floor=2. For n=5, (7)/2=3.5, floor=3. So m ≥ ⌊(n+2)/2⌋. That's equal to ⌈n/2⌉ + 1? Actually for n=4, ⌈4/2⌉=2, +1 =3. For n=3, ⌈3/2⌉=2, +1=3, but we have m≥2, so that's not necessary. So that's not a universal bound.

Better approach: Prove that k ≤ ⌊(n-1)/2⌋. This is an upper bound. We need to show that if there are more than ⌊(n-1)/2⌋ sunny lines, then covering cannot be achieved. This is equivalent to showing that m ≤ n - ⌊(n-1)/2⌋ = ⌈(n+1)/2⌉? Actually n - ⌊(n-1)/2⌋ = ⌈(n+1)/2⌉? Let's compute: n=3 → n - 1 =2, m ≤2; n=4 → n -1 =3, m ≤3; n=5 → n -2 =3, m ≤3; n=6 → n -2 =4, m ≤4. So m ≤ something like ⌈n/2⌉? Not exactly. Actually we can try to prove that the number of non-sunny lines must be at least ⌈(n+1)/2⌉? Let's test: n=3 → ⌈4/2⌉=2, matches m≥2. n=4 → ⌈5/2⌉=3, matches m≥3. n=5 → ⌈6/2⌉=3, matches m≥3. n=6 → ⌈7/2⌉=4, matches m≥4. So it seems m ≥ ⌈(n+1)/2⌉? For n=3, ⌈4/2⌉=2; n=4, ⌈5/2⌉=3; n=5, ⌈6/2⌉=3; n=6, ⌈7/2⌉=4. That's a pattern. Is it always true that any covering of P_n by n lines must have at least ⌈(n+1)/2⌉ non-sunny lines? Let's check n=3: we have m=2 (x=1,y=1) covering 5 points, then sunny line y=x covering the remaining 1 point; that's 2 non-sunny, 1 sunny. So m=2, which is ⌈(3+1)/2⌉ = 2. That holds. For n=4, we have a covering with 3 non-sunny lines (x=1,y=1,x+y=5) and 1 sunny (y=x) but we saw that's not covering all points? Actually we need to check if there exists a covering with m=3 and k=1. That's the example we gave: lines L1: x=1, L2: y=1, L3: x+y=5, L4: y=x. That's 1 sunny, 3 non-sunny, covering all points. So m=3. ⌈(4+1)/2⌉ = ⌈5/2⌉ = 3. That matches. For n=5, is there a covering with m=3 and k=2? That would be m=3, k=2, total 5. But we need to see if such covering exists. According to the bound we suspect, m must be at least ⌈(n+1)/2⌉ = 3, so that's allowed. But we also need to see whether k=2 is achievable. If the bound is necessary, it doesn't guarantee that k=2 is achievable; we need to construct. So the bound is m ≥ ⌈(n+1)/2⌉. That would be equivalent to k ≤ n - ⌈(n+1)/2⌉ = ⌊(n-1)/2⌋. So indeed, the upper bound on k is ⌊(n-1)/2⌋. So if we can prove that in any covering of P_n by n distinct lines, the number of non-sunny lines is at least ⌈(n+1)/2⌉, then k ≤ n - ⌈(n+1)/2⌉ = ⌊(n-1)/2⌋. That's exactly what we want.

Now, we need to prove that lower bound on m. How to prove that? Let's attempt.

We have n lines. Let’s denote the set of non-sunny lines as N (size m). They are lines with slopes 0, ∞, or -1. For each such line, we can consider the points it covers. However, we need to use the fact that the lines are distinct and there are exactly n lines. Perhaps we can consider the points of P_n with small coordinates, and argue that many of them cannot be covered by sunny lines alone. Or we can use an averaging or pigeonhole principle on the "triangular" structure.

Another idea: Since sunny lines cannot be horizontal or vertical, they cannot cover points with a=1 or b=1 in a "coordinate" way? Actually they can cover points like (1,2) if they pass through that point. But the line through (1,2) and (2,4) is slope 2, which is sunny. So that's fine. So the difficulty is not about covering points on the axes.

Maybe we can consider the points where both coordinates are at least 2. Those are a triangle of size (n-2). The number of such points is (n-1)(n-2)/2. Suppose we have only a few non-sunny lines. Then most of these points must be covered by sunny lines. But as we saw, each sunny line can cover at most ⌊(n+1)/2⌋ points. But we have (n - m) sunny lines. So the maximum number of points they can cover in the interior is at most (n - m) * ⌊(n+1)/2⌋. However, the interior points are only (n-1)(n-2)/2. So we need:

(n - m) * ⌊(n+1)/2⌋ ≥ (n-1)(n-2)/2.

If this fails, then any covering must have more non-sunny lines. So we can get a lower bound on m. But is this inequality always true for m small? Let's test with n=4. Then ⌊(n+1)/2⌋ = ⌊5/2⌋ = 2. (n - m) = 4 - m. (n-1)(n-2)/2 = 3*2/2 = 3. So inequality becomes (4 - m)*2 ≥ 3 → 8 - 2m ≥ 3 → 2m ≤ 5 → m ≤ 2.5 → m ≤ 2. So if m ≤ 2, then inequality holds (since (4-2)*2 = 4 ≥ 3). But we need to see whether m=2 is possible? That would be k=2, which we already argued impossible. So this inequality doesn't rule out m=2; it's satisfied. But we also need to consider that the sunny lines might not be able to cover all interior points because they might not be arranged to cover exactly those points. However, we are assuming that the covering exists. So if m=2, then (n - m) = 2 sunny lines. The maximum number of interior points they can cover (distinct) is at most 2 * ⌊(n+1)/2⌋ = 4, but there are 3 interior points. So it's possible that 2 sunny lines cover all 3 interior points. But we argued that in any configuration, the interior points (a,b≥2) after covering with non-sunny lines would be a set of size at least something. But we need to derive a contradiction. So this approach is insufficient.

Maybe we should consider the points with a+b = n+1 (hypotenuse). There are n points. Each sunny line can cover at most 1. So to cover these n points, we need at least (n - k) non-sunny lines if we assume that the sunny lines cover at most k points? Actually if we have k sunny lines, they can cover at most k points on the hypotenuse (since each covers at most 1). The remaining n - k points must be covered by non-sunny lines. But a non-sunny line can cover all n points (if it's x+y = n+1). So we don't need many non-sunny lines. So that doesn't give a bound.

Maybe we need to consider the "sunny" condition more carefully: A line is sunny if it is not parallel to any of the x-axis, y-axis, or x+y=0. So it cannot have slope 0, undefined, or -1. That's it. So lines with other slopes are sunny. So we can use lines with slope 1 (y=x + c) and also slope 2, 1/2, etc. But note that slope 1 lines are sunny only if they are not parallel to x+y=0, but they are not parallel to that line (since slope 1 vs -1). So all slope 1 lines are sunny. So we have many sunny lines. The maximum number of points a sunny line can cover within P_n is ⌊(n+1)/2⌋. That's about n/2. So if we have many sunny lines, they can cover a large set, but perhaps they cannot cover all points in the triangle because of some combinatorial constraints.

Let's think about covering with lines. The problem is reminiscent of "cover all points in a triangular grid with lines, where some lines are forbidden". Usually, such problems ask to find the minimal number of lines needed. But here we have exactly n lines, and we want to determine possible numbers of sunny lines. The bound might come from considering the "inversions" or "pairings" of points with respect to lines. Another idea: Use a double counting argument with pairs (point, line) where the point lies on the line. For each point, it must be covered by at least one line. So total incidences (point-line pairs) is at least N_n. On the other hand, each line can cover at most some number of points. For non-sunny lines, we can bound the maximum number of points they can cover by n (since they are of types H,V,D). For sunny lines, we can bound the maximum number of points they can cover by ⌊(n+1)/2⌋. So if we have m non-sunny lines and k sunny lines, then the total incidences is at most m * n + k * ⌊(n+1)/2⌋. Since we need at least N_n incidences, we have:

m * n + k * ⌊(n+1)/2⌋ ≥ N_n = n(n+1)/2.

But note that m = n - k. So substitute: (n - k) * n + k * ⌊(n+1)/2⌋ ≥ n(n+1)/2.

Simplify: n^2 - nk + k * ⌊(n+1)/2⌋ ≥ n(n+1)/2.

We want to find the maximum k such that this inequality holds. If it fails, then any covering would be impossible. However, the inequality is a necessary condition, but is it also sufficient for existence? Not necessarily, but if we can prove that if k is too large, then the inequality cannot hold, then such k are impossible. So we can get an upper bound on k.

Let's compute:

(n - k) n + k ⌊(n+1)/2⌋ = n^2 - nk + k ⌊(n+1)/2⌋ = n^2 + k (⌊(n+1)/2⌋ - n).

So we need:

n^2 + k (⌊(n+1)/2⌋ - n) ≥ n(n+1)/2.

Now, ⌊(n+1)/2⌋ is roughly n/2. So ⌊(n+1)/2⌋ - n is about -n/2. So the left side is n^2 minus about (n/2)k. The right side is about n^2/2. So the inequality becomes:

n^2 - (something) k ≥ n^2/2 → (something) k ≤ n^2/2 → k ≤ (n^2/2) / (something). But we need to solve precisely.

Let’s denote:

A = ⌊(n+1)/2⌋. Then inequality:

n^2 - nk + k A ≥ n(n+1)/2.

=> n^2 + k(A - n) ≥ n(n+1)/2.

=> k(A - n) ≥ n(n+1)/2 - n^2 = (n^2 + n - 2n^2)/2 = (-n^2 + n)/2 = n(1 - n)/2 = -n(n-1)/2.

Since A - n is negative. Multiply both sides by -1 (reversing inequality):

k (n - A) ≤ n(n-1)/2.

Thus:

k ≤ \frac{n(n-1)}{2(n - A)}.

Now, compute n - A. A = ⌊(n+1)/2⌋.

We need to compute n - ⌊(n+1)/2⌋.

Case n even: n = 2t. Then n+1 = 2t+1, ⌊(2t+1)/2⌋ = t. So A = t. Then n - A = 2t - t = t. So n - A = t = n/2.

Thus k ≤ \frac{n(n-1)}{2 * (n/2)} = \frac{n(n-1)}{n} = n-1.

So we get k ≤ n-1, which is trivial (k ≤ n). That doesn't give a strong bound. For n odd: n = 2t+1. Then n+1 = 2t+2, ⌊(2t+2)/2⌋ = t+1. So A = t+1. Then n - A = (2t+1) - (t+1) = t. So n - A = t = (n-1)/2. Then k ≤ \frac{n(n-1)}{2 * ((n-1)/2)} = \frac{n(n-1)}{n-1} = n. So again trivial. So this inequality gives no useful bound. That's because we used the maximum number of points a sunny line can cover, but we also used that non-sunny lines can cover up to n points. However, the bound m * n + k * A is an upper bound on incidences, but we need a lower bound. So if the upper bound is less than N_n, then impossible. That's what we did. But we got trivial bound because the upper bound is large enough. So that doesn't restrict k.

Thus we need a different approach. Perhaps we need to consider the "sunny" condition and prove that the number of sunny lines cannot exceed something like ⌊(n-1)/2⌋. That suggests that if we have too many sunny lines, then the total number of points covered by sunny lines (given that each sunny line covers at most something) is insufficient, but we also have non-sunny lines that can cover many points. But the inequality we derived was an upper bound on incidences, not a lower bound. To get a contradiction, we need to show that if k is large, then the total incidences (point-line pairs) from the set of lines must be at least N_n, but the maximum possible incidences from those lines is less than N_n. That's a typical necessary condition. However, we used upper bounds on each line's coverage. That gave us: total incidences ≤ m * n + k * A. We then required that this ≥ N_n. But we found that this inequality holds for many k. Actually we solved for k ≤ something and got a weak bound. But we want to prove impossibility for large k, so we need to show that for large k, this inequality cannot hold. But we got that it holds for k ≤ n-1 (which is all). So that approach fails.

We need to find a stronger upper bound on the number of points a sunny line can cover. Perhaps we can prove that a sunny line can cover at most ⌊(n+1)/2⌋ points, but that's already used. That bound is not strong enough. So we need to incorporate the fact that the lines are distinct and that there are exactly n lines. But maybe we can prove that each non-sunny line can cover at most n points, but that's not a restriction. The problem seems to ask for a characterization of possible k. Perhaps the answer is that k can be any integer from 0 to n, except that k = n-1 and k = n are impossible. But we saw n=4, k=2 impossible, so that would be k = n-2 impossible as well. That's not a simple exception. Let's test n=5 with a possible construction. If k=2 is achievable, then we need to construct with 5 lines, 2 sunny, 3 non-sunny. The non-sunny lines could be, say, y=1 (covers 5 points), x=1 (covers 5 points), and x+y=6 (covers 5 points). But we already saw that union of those three lines is 12 points, leaving 3 points uncovered. Then with 2 sunny lines, we need to cover those 3 points. But maybe we can choose a different set of 3 non-sunny lines that cover all points. Could there be a triple that covers all 15 points? That would be a covering with 3 lines. But we argued max union is 12. So impossible. So k=2 is not achievable by that triple, but perhaps we can use a different combination of non-sunny lines (maybe not all three of the maximum coverage) and sunny lines that cover points not covered by non-sunny lines, but we have 3 non-sunny lines and 2 sunny lines. The union size from 3 non-sunny lines can be up to 12. The remaining points (at least 3) must be covered by sunny lines. Since sunny lines are only 2, they need to cover at least 3 points. But can 2 sunny lines cover 3 points that are not covered by non-sunny lines? Possibly yes if those points are not all pairwise non-sunny. However, the uncovered set is the complement of the union. The complement size is at least 3. The complement points might be such that some pair lies on a sunny line. But we need to see if it's possible to arrange the non-sunny lines such that the complement points are, say, {(2,2),(2,3),(3,3)} (but (3,3) is in P_n for n=5? 3+3=6 ≤6, so yes). Then we could cover (2,2) and (3,3) with a sunny line (y=x). And (2,3) could be covered by another sunny line. So that would be possible. So we need to check whether there exists a selection of 3 non-sunny lines that leaves exactly those 3 points uncovered (or a set of size ≤3 that can be covered by 2 sunny lines). The complement size is N_n - |union|. To have complement size ≤2, we need union size ≥13. That's impossible because max union is 12. So complement size ≥3. So we need to find a triple of non-sunny lines whose union has size 12, and such that the complement (3 points) can be covered by 2 sunny lines. The complement will be a set of 3 points. If any two of those points lie on a sunny line, then we can cover that pair with one sunny line, and the third with another. That's possible. So we need to see if there exists a triple of non-sunny lines with union size 12, and complement points not all pairwise non-sunny. For n=5, we examined some triples and got complements like (2,2),(2,3),(3,2) or (1,4),(2,4),(1,5) etc. Those are all "bad". But maybe we can choose a different triple, like using two lines of slope -1 and one horizontal? Let's enumerate all possible non-sunny lines (horizontal, vertical, slope -1). For n=5, the points are (a,b) with a,b≥1, a+b≤6. The non-sunny lines are:

Horizontal: H_c = { (a,c) : a ≥ 1, a + c ≤ 6 }.
Vertical: V_d = { (d,b) : b ≥ 1, d + b ≤ 6 }.
Slope -1: D_e = { (a,b) : a + b = e }.

Now, we want to choose 3 lines such that the union covers as many points as possible, i.e., 12. Many triples achieve 12. Let's compute the complement for each candidate triple and see if any complement has a sunny pair. But maybe it's impossible for any triple to have a complement that is not a "bad" triple. We need to prove that if there are at least (n - ⌊(n-1)/2⌋) non-sunny lines (i.e., m ≥ ⌈(n+1)/2⌉), then covering is possible, but we are looking for upper bound on k. So we need to show that if k > ⌊(n-1)/2⌋, then covering cannot be achieved. So we need to prove that any covering must have m ≥ ⌈(n+1)/2⌉. That's a lower bound on m. So we need to derive that m cannot be too small. That is, if we have too few non-sunny lines, then there are too many points that cannot be covered by the remaining sunny lines (or that the sunny lines cannot cover all points because of some structural constraints). This seems plausible. Let's attempt to prove that.

We have n lines. Let’s denote the set of sunny lines as S (size k), non-sunny as N (size m). We need to cover P_n. Consider the points with a+b = n+1 (hypotenuse). There are n points. We need to find a more refined necessary condition that forces many non-sunny lines. Let's think about the structure of covering. The points are in a right triangle. We need n lines. Suppose we take ⌊(n-1)/2⌋ sunny lines, and the rest are non-sunny. The non-sunny lines will be of types that cover many points. For n even, say n = 2t. Let’s define:

- Total points N_n.
- Interior points I_n = {(a,b) : a,b ≥ 2, a+b ≤ n+1}. Size |I_n| = (n-1)(n-2)/2.

Now, each line L (non-sunny or sunny) can cover at most some number of interior points. But we can bound the maximum interior points per line.

For a non-sunny line:
- Horizontal y = c. For points with a,b≥2, we need b = c ≥ 2. Also a ≥ 2 and a + c ≤ n+1 ⇒ a ≤ n+1 - c. So the number of interior points on H_c is at most (n+1 - c) - 2 + 1 = n - c. Actually a from 2 to n+1 - c inclusive, count = (n+1 - c) - 2 + 1 = n - c. So H_c covers at most n - c interior points. The maximum occurs at c=2, giving n-2 interior points. So non-sunny lines are efficient at covering interior points, but each covers at most n-2.

For a sunny line, we need to bound the maximum number of interior points it can contain. As we saw, a sunny line with slope 1 (y=x) covers (⌊(n+1)/2⌋ - 1) interior points. That's about n/2 - 1. For a sunny line with slope 2, it covers fewer. So the maximum interior points per sunny line is achieved by slope 1. So max interior points per sunny line is ⌊(n+1)/2⌋ - 1 (if n+1 is even, that's (n+1)/2 - 1 = (n-1)/2; if n+1 is odd, that's (n)/2 - 1? Actually compute: n=5 → n+1=6 → ⌊6/2⌋=3 → interior points = 3-1=2. That's (n-1)/2? (5-1)/2=2. So indeed, for odd n, (n-1)/2. For n=4, n+1=5 → ⌊5/2⌋=2 → interior points = 2-1=1. That's (n-2)/2? Actually (4-2)/2=1. So it's floor((n-1)/2) maybe. Let's compute precisely:

Let A = ⌊(n+1)/2⌋. Then number of points on y=x with a=b and a+b ≤ n+1 is A (since a ≤ A). Among those, a≥2 gives max(0, A-1). So interior points from y=x is max(0, A-1). That's ⌊(n+1)/2⌋ - 1. So that's about n/2 - 1. For n even, n=2t, then A = t, interior = t-1 = n/2 - 1. For n odd, n=2t+1, A = t+1, interior = t = (n-1)/2. So it's roughly n/2 - 1.

Now, the total number of interior points is (n-1)(n-2)/2. If we have m non-sunny lines, they can cover at most m * (n-2) interior points. The sunny lines (k of them) can cover at most k * (A-1) interior points. So we have:

m * (n-2) + k * (A-1) ≥ |I_n| = (n-1)(n-2)/2.   (1)

Since m = n - k. Substitute:

(n - k) (n-2) + k (A-1) ≥ (n-1)(n-2)/2.

Now, compute (n - k)(n-2) = n(n-2) - k(n-2). So left side = n(n-2) - k(n-2) + k(A-1) = n(n-2) + k( (A-1) - (n-2) ) = n(n-2) + k( A - n + 1 ).

Now, A - n is negative. So we have:

n(n-2) + k (A - n + 1) ≥ (n-1)(n-2)/2.

We can attempt to see whether this inequality forces a lower bound on m, i.e., an upper bound on k. Solve for k.

Compute (n-1)(n-2)/2 = (n^2 - 3n + 2)/2.

n(n-2) = n^2 - 2n.

So inequality:

n^2 - 2n + k (A - n + 1) ≥ (n^2 - 3n + 2)/2.

Multiply both sides by 2:

2n^2 - 4n + 2k (A - n + 1) ≥ n^2 - 3n + 2.

Bring terms:

2n^2 - 4n - n^2 + 3n - 2 ≥ -2k (A - n + 1) => (n^2 - n - 2) ≥ -2k (A - n + 1).

Actually compute:

2n^2 - 4n + 2k (A - n + 1) - (n^2 - 3n + 2) ≥ 0 => (2n^2 - n^2) + (-4n + 3n) + ( -2) + 2k (A - n + 1) ≥ 0 => n^2 - n - 2 + 2k (A - n + 1) ≥ 0.

So 2k (A - n + 1) ≥ -(n^2 - n - 2) = -n^2 + n + 2.

Thus k (A - n + 1) ≥ (-n^2 + n + 2)/2.

Now, A - n + 1 = ⌊(n+1)/2⌋ - n + 1. Let's compute:

Case n even: n = 2t. Then A = t. So A - n + 1 = t - 2t + 1 = 1 - t = -(t-1). That's negative. So left side is negative. Right side is (-n^2 + n + 2)/2, which is negative for large n. So this inequality is something like k * (negative) ≥ negative. That could be satisfied for many k. Let's solve:

k * (A - n + 1) ≥ \frac{-n^2 + n + 2}{2}.

Since A - n + 1 is negative, dividing by it flips inequality. So we get:

k ≤ \frac{-n^2 + n + 2}{2 (A - n + 1)}.

But A - n + 1 is negative, so the denominator is negative, making the fraction something like? Let's compute numeric for n=4: t=2, A=2, A-n+1 = 2-4+1 = -1. -n^2+n+2 = -16+4+2 = -10. Then k ≤ (-10) / (2 * (-1)) = (-10) / (-2) = 5. That's trivial. So this doesn't bound.

But we want a lower bound on m, so perhaps we need a different invariant.

Let's think about the geometry. The problem is from some contest? Possibly it's about "sunny lines" and covering lattice points. For n=3, ⌈3/2⌉=2, +1=3, but we have m=2. So that's not consistent. So we need a proof that works for all n≥3, with the lower bound being ⌈(n+1)/2⌉. For n=3, that's 2. For n=4, that's 3. For n=5, that's 3. So that pattern is: m ≥ ⌈(n+1)/2⌉. That's equivalent to m ≥ ⌊n/2⌋ + 1 when n is even? Let's compute:

n=3 → ⌈4/2⌉=2 → ⌊3/2⌋+1 =1+1=2 → works.
n=4 → ⌈5/2⌉=3 → ⌊4/2⌋+1=2+1=3 → works.
n=5 → ⌈6/2⌉=3 → ⌊5/2⌋+1=2+1=3 → works.
So indeed, m ≥ ⌊n/2⌋ + 1.

Thus, we need to prove that any covering of P_n by n lines must have at least ⌊n/2⌋ + 1 non-sunny lines. That's a stronger statement than m ≥ ⌈(n+1)/2⌉? Let's see: For n=3, ⌊3/2⌋+1 = 2, which is ⌈4/2⌉ = 2. For n=4, ⌊4/2⌋+1 = 3, which is ⌈5/2⌉ = 3. For n=5, ⌊5/2⌋+1 = 3, which is ⌈6/2⌉ = 3. So they coincide. So it's fine.

Now, how to prove that? Let's attempt a combinatorial argument using pairs of points.

Observation: The points of P_n can be partitioned into "layers" based on a+b. The number of points in layer s is s-1, for s=2..n+1. So total N_n. - For i = 1,2,..., t where t = something like ⌈n/2⌉? Actually we need to cover all points. Perhaps we can take the following non-sunny lines:

Idea: Consider the points (a,b) with a,b ≥ 2. There are I_n = (n-1)(n-2)/2. For each such point, it must lie on some line. For n=4, B = 1; for n=5, B = 2. So it's roughly n/2 - 1.

Now, we also have non-sunny lines. They can cover interior points. But the number of non-sunny lines is m = n - k. We want to derive a contradiction if k is too large (i.e., m is too small). So we assume k > ⌊(n-1)/2⌋. Then m = n - k < n - ⌊(n-1)/2⌋ = ⌈(n+1)/2⌉? Actually let's compute:

|I_n| ≤ m * (n-2) + k * B.

Now, |I_n| = (n-1)(n-2)/2.

So inequality:

(n-1)(n-2)/2 ≤ m (n-2) + k B.

Since m = n - k, substitute:

(n-1)(n-2)/2 ≤ (n - k)(n-2) + k B.

Simplify:

(n-1)(n-2)/2 ≤ (n-2)n - k (n-2) + k B = n(n-2) + k (B - n + 2).

Thus:

k (B - n + 2) ≥ (n-1)(n-2)/2 - n(n-2).

Compute RHS:

n(n-2) = n^2 - 2n.
(n-1)(n-2)/2 = (n^2 - 3n + 2)/2.
So difference: (n-1)(n-2)/2 - n(n-2) = \frac{n^2 - 3n + 2 - 2n^2 + 4n}{2} = \frac{-n^2 + n + 2}{2} = \frac{-(n^2 - n - 2)}{2}.

So RHS = \frac{-(n^2 - n - 2)}{2}.

Now, B - n + 2 = (⌊(n+1)/2⌋ - 1) - n + 2 = ⌊(n+1)/2⌋ - n + 1.

(4,2)

Now, which lines can cover them? Non-sunny lines are of types H,V,D. Let's consider covering all interior points with three lines. That seems plausible. For example, we could take:

- L1: y = 1 (horizontal) covers (a,1) but no interior because b=1. So not helpful.
- L2: x = 2 (vertical) covers (2,b) with b up to 4. That's (2,2),(2,3),(2,4). That's 3 interior points.
- L3: y = 2 (horizontal) covers (a,2) with a up to 4. That's (2,2),(3,2),(4,2). That's 3 interior points.
- L4: x = 3 (vertical) covers (3,b) with b up to 3. That's (3,2),(3,3). That's 2 interior points.
- L5: x = 4 (vertical) covers (4,2) only.
- L6: x+y = 4 covers (2,2),(3,1) but (3,1) not interior, (2,2) is interior. So 1 interior.
- L7: x+y = 5 covers (2,3),(3,2) -> 2 interior.
- L8: x+y = 6 covers (2,4),(3,3),(4,2) -> 3 interior.
So we can cover interior points with various lines. To cover all 6 interior points with 3 lines, we need to select three lines such that each interior point lies on at least one. The set of interior points is like a smaller triangle (since a,b≥2 and a+b≤6). It's a triangle with vertices (2,2),(2,4),(4,2). That's a triangle of lattice points with coordinates ≥2. The number of interior points is 6. The lines that are non-sunny can be used. The question: can we cover this triangle (size 3) with 3 lines of types H,V,D? The triangle's points are (2,2),(2,3),(2,4),(3,2),(3,3),(4,2). To cover all, we could use:

- L1: x=2 covers (2,2),(2,3),(2,4) → 3 points.
- L2: y=2 covers (2,2),(3,2),(4,2) → 3 points.
- L3: x+y=5 (or 6) covers (2,3),(3,2) → 2 points. But we still have (3,3) uncovered. So need a line covering (3,3). That could be x+y=6, which covers (3,3). So we could have L1: x=2, L2: y=2, L3: x+y=6. That covers all: (2,2) covered by L1 and L2, (2,3) covered by L1 and L3, (2,4) covered by L1 and L3? Actually (2,4) sum=6 → covered by L3. (3,2) covered by L2 and L3. (3,3) covered by L3. (4,2) covered by L2 and L3. So indeed, with 3 non-sunny lines (x=2, y=2, x+y=6), we cover all interior points. But we also need to cover the points on the axes. The points on axes are those with a=1 or b=1. Those are covered by the lines x=1 or y=1, but we didn't include them. However, we have total n lines = 5. If we use these 3 non-sunny lines, we still have 2 lines left (sunnny). Those can cover the axes points. But we need to ensure that all points are covered. The points on axes are many. Can 2 sunny lines cover all points with a=1 or b=1? Let's compute.

- L1 (x=2) covers (2,b) with b up to 4. That's not axes (a=2,b≥1), but (2,1) is covered by L1? Actually (2,1) has a=2, b=1, so it's covered by L1 (since x=2). That's a point on the b=1 axis. Thus, it seems that covering all interior points with non-sunny lines doesn't guarantee covering all axes points. The axes points are many. But we have 2 sunny lines left. Could 2 sunny lines cover all axes points? Possibly if they are chosen appropriately. But we saw that with n=5, axes points total 9. The uncovered axes points after L1,L2,L3 are (1,1),(1,3),(1,4),(3,1),(4,1). That's 5. But we also have points like (1,2) and (1,5) and (2,1) etc covered. So we need to cover 5 points with 2 sunny lines. That's plausible if those points are collinear with a sunny line. But they are not all collinear. However, maybe we can choose different non-sunny lines such that the uncovered set is smaller, or such that the sunny lines can cover them. The bound we want is k ≤ ⌊(n-1)/2⌋. So for n=5, k ≤ 2. That suggests that any covering with 3 sunny lines (k=3) is impossible. So we need to prove that if k ≥ 3, then covering is impossible. So we need a proof that relies on counting something like "the number of points on the axes that cannot be covered by non-sunny lines" and "the maximum number of such points that can be covered by sunny lines" is limited. Let's explore.

We have n lines. Let m = n - k be non-sunny lines. We want to prove that m ≥ ⌈(n+1)/2⌉. That's a lower bound. So we assume m ≤ ⌈(n+1)/2⌉ - 1, i.e., m ≤ ⌊n/2⌋. Then k ≥ n - ⌊n/2⌋ = ⌈n/2⌉. That's a large number. So we need to show that such a covering cannot exist.

Perhaps we can argue by considering the points (1,1), (1,2), ..., (1,n) and (2,1), (3,1), ..., (n,1). That's 2n-1 points. These are the "edge" points. For each such point, it must lie on some line. A sunny line can intersect the set of these edge points in at most something. But we have many sunny lines. However, we can also use non-sunny lines to cover them. The idea is to bound the total number of edge points that can be covered by the m non-sunny lines. Since each non-sunny line is either horizontal, vertical, or slope -1. A horizontal line y = c can cover points with that y-coordinate, including (a,c) where a≥1. That's an edge point if c is small? Actually edge points are those with a=1 or b=1. A horizontal line y = c will cover points (a,c) for a≥1. For c=1, it covers all (a,1) which are edge points (b=1). For c=2, it covers (a,2) where a≥1. Among those, only (1,2) is an edge point (a=1). So a horizontal line can cover at most one edge point from the a=1 column, and many from the b=1 row if c=1. Similarly, a vertical line x = d covers points (d,b) for b≥1. For d=1, it covers all (1,b) which are edge points (a=1). For d=2, it covers (2,b) where (2,1) is edge point (b=1). So each non-sunny line can cover many edge points. But we need to bound the total number of edge points that can be covered by m non-sunny lines. Since edge points are many, maybe we can prove that if m is too small, then the number of edge points that can be covered by non-sunny lines is insufficient, and the sunny lines cannot cover the remaining edge points because each sunny line can cover at most something like ⌊n/2⌋ edge points. Let's compute.

Edge points E_n = {(a,b): a≥1,b≥1, a+b ≤ n+1, (a=1 or b=1)}. Size = 2n-1. They are all points with a=1 or b=1. Some points are both (1,1). So 2n-1 distinct points.

Now, each line L (non-sunny or sunny) can contain at most some number of edge points. Let's bound for a sunny line L. If L is sunny, it's not parallel to x-axis, y-axis, or x+y=0. So it can have slope s ≠ 0, ∞, -1. Consider the edge points. The edge points are all points with a=1 (column) or b=1 (row). A line that is not horizontal or vertical can intersect the column a=1 at at most one point (since a=1 determines a vertical line, but a line with slope not infinite will intersect the line a=1 at exactly one point). Similarly, it can intersect the row b=1 at at most one point. So a sunny line can contain at most 2 edge points (one on a=1, one on b=1). However, is it possible that a sunny line contains a point that is on both a=1 and b=1 (i.e., (1,1))? That would be one point. So max edge points per sunny line is 2. That's a strong bound.

Now, for non-sunny lines, they can contain many edge points. For example, the line x=1 (vertical) contains all points (1,b) with b≤n, which are n edge points (a=1). Similarly, y=1 (horizontal) contains n edge points (b=1). A line of slope -1, say x+y = n+1, contains points with a+b = n+1. Among those, the edge points are those where a=1 or b=1. That's (1,n) and (n,1). So that's 2 edge points. So the most efficient non-sunny lines for covering edge points are the vertical and horizontal lines. So if we have m non-sunny lines, they can cover at most something like 2m? But they can be chosen to be vertical/horizontal. However, note that we have n lines total, and we can decide which are non-sunny. To minimize the number of non-sunny lines, we might want to use only non-sunny lines that cover many edge points. So if m is small, the total number of edge points that can be covered by non-sunny lines is at most (something) times m. But we also have sunny lines that can cover up to 2 edge points each. So we can bound the total edge points coverage.

Now, consider the edge points E_n. They must be covered by the n lines. The sum over lines of |L ∩ E_n| ≥ |E_n| = 2n-1.

We have m non-sunny lines and k sunny lines. Let's denote:

- For each non-sunny line L, |L ∩ E_n| ≤ n (if it's x=1 or y=1), but also could be less if it's something else. But we can bound it by n. However, to get a contradiction when m is small, we can assume that the non-sunny lines are chosen to maximize edge coverage, but they must also cover interior points. But we can use an upper bound on the total edge coverage that is possible with m non-sunny lines, given that they are distinct lines. But we need a lower bound on the coverage required. Since the required coverage is 2n-1, and the maximum coverage from sunny lines is k * 2. So if m is small, the total coverage from non-sunny lines must be at least (2n-1) - 2k. But we also have an upper bound on coverage from non-sunny lines: each non-sunny line can cover at most n edge points. That's not restrictive. However, we can also consider that non-sunny lines are limited in number, and they cannot all be vertical/horizontal because they must be distinct lines. For example, we could have vertical lines x=1, x=2, ..., x=m. Those each cover some edge points. The edge points on column a=1 are n points (1,b). The vertical line x=1 covers all n. The vertical line x=2 covers only (2,1) (edge point) because (2,1) is b=1; (2,b) for b≥2 are not edge points (since a=2, b≥2 => not a=1 nor b=1). So the number of edge points on x=2 is 1 (if b=1) plus maybe (2,1) is covered, but also (2,? Actually edge points with b=1 are (a,1) for a≥1. So vertical line x=2 covers (2,1) which is an edge point. So for a vertical line x=d, the edge points it covers are: (d,1) if d≥1 (b=1) and also (d,? Actually if d=1, it covers all (1,b) with b≤n, which are edge points because a=1. For d>1, it covers (d,1) only, and (d,? maybe (d,1) is edge point. Also if d=1, it covers (1,b) for b≤n, but also (1,1) counted. So coverage from vertical lines: x=1 gives n edge points; x=2 gives 1; x=3 gives 1; ... up to x=n gives 1. So total edge points from m vertical lines (assuming we pick the first m) would be n + (m-1). Similarly, horizontal lines: y=1 gives n edge points; y=2 gives 1; etc. So we can cover many edge points with few non-sunny lines by using vertical/horizontal lines.

But we have to cover all points. The problem is to determine all possible k. Perhaps we can prove that k ≤ ⌊(n-1)/2⌋ by a simple pairing argument: For each sunny line, we can consider some kind of "inversion" with respect to the triangle. Another approach: Consider the set of points (a,b) with a+b = n+1 (hypotenuse). There are n points. Each sunny line can cover at most 1 of them. So if we have k sunny lines, they can cover at most k hypotenuse points. The remaining n - k hypotenuse points must be covered by non-sunny lines. That's fine. But maybe we can consider the points (a,b) with a+b = n (the next layer). There are n-1 points. Sunny lines can cover at most 1 each, so they can cover at most k points in that layer. The non-sunny lines can also cover points in that layer. But the total points in that layer is n-1. So if k is large, then the number of points in that layer covered by sunny lines is at most k, but they might cover many. However, we need to cover all points. So if k is large, then the number of non-sunny lines is small, and they must cover many points. But we can bound the maximum number of points that can be covered by m non-sunny lines in the triangle. Perhaps we can use a double counting of incidences of lines with points, but with a more precise bound on the maximum number of points a non-sunny line can cover (which is n), and a more precise bound on the maximum number of points a sunny line can cover (which is ⌊(n+1)/2⌋). The total number of points is about n^2/2. If m is small, then the total coverage capacity of all lines (upper bound) is at most m*n + k*⌊(n+1)/2⌋. But we already considered that. However, note that the upper bound on coverage per line is not the maximum possible points on that line within P_n, but it's an upper bound. That's what we used. The inequality N_n ≤ m*n + k*A holds. This inequality is a necessary condition. It is not sufficient, but if it fails, impossible. So to prove impossibility for large k, we need to show that N_n > m*n + k*A. Let's test for n=4, k=2 (which we suspect impossible). m=2, A=⌊5/2⌋=2. Then m*n + k*A = 2*4 + 2*2 = 8+4=12. N_n=10. So inequality holds (10 ≤ 12). So that doesn't rule out. For n=4, k=3 (m=1) gives 1*4 + 3*2 = 4+6=10, N_n=10, holds. So that inequality doesn't forbid k=3. But we know that for n=4, k=3 is impossible. So we need a stronger condition. Perhaps we need to consider that not only the total coverage must be at least N_n, but also that each point must be covered by at least one line. The upper bound we used is an upper bound on the sum of coverage. If that upper bound is less than N_n, then impossible. If it's greater or equal, it's not a contradiction. So we need a different necessary condition, maybe involving the fact that some points cannot be covered by any sunny line if the number of sunny lines is too high because of some combinatorial limitation? That seems unlikely.

Let's attempt to prove the upper bound directly by considering the points with a+b = n+1 and a+b = n. Suppose we have k sunny lines. For each sunny line, it can contain at most 1 point from the set H (hypotenuse). So the sunny lines cover at most k points of H. The non-sunny lines can cover at most m*n points of H, but actually a non-sunny line can cover at most n points of H (if it's x=1 or y=1, but H is the set of points with a+b=n+1. A vertical line x=d intersects H at (d, n+1-d) if that point is within bounds. So it covers at most 1 point of H. Similarly, a horizontal line y=d covers at most 1 point. A slope -1 line x+y = n+1 covers all n points. So the most efficient non-sunny line for covering H is a slope -1 line, which covers all n. So if we have m non-sunny lines, they could cover at most m*n points of H? But that's an overestimate. Actually each non-sunny line can cover at most n points of H (if it's a slope -1 line), but if we use many non-sunny lines, they might be of types that cover at most 1 each. So we need to bound the total coverage of H by the lines. The total points in H is n. The coverage of H from sunny lines is at most k. So the remaining points in H (n - k) must be covered by non-sunny lines. So we need m non-sunny lines such that they can cover those n - k points. That is always possible if we include a line x+y = n+1. So that doesn't bound k.

Now, perhaps we need to consider points with a+b = n (second layer). There are n-1 points. Sunny lines can cover at most 1 each, so at most k. The non-sunny lines can cover at most m*n points of that layer, but again not restrictive. However, note that the points in that layer are also edge points? Some are on axes. For example, (1,n-1) is on a=1 axis, (n-1,1) is on b=1 axis. So they are edge points. So they are part of the edge set E_n. So if we consider edge points, we have a bound of 2 per sunny line. That's strong. The edge points are 2n-1. So we can write:

2n-1 ≤ 2k + m * something. But m * something is an upper bound on edge coverage from non-sunny lines. Since each non-sunny line can cover at most n edge points (if it's x=1 or y=1). But we can also have non-sunny lines that cover many edge points. However, if m is small, then the maximum edge coverage from non-sunny lines is at most m * n. But that's not a contradiction. But perhaps we can prove a stronger bound: Each non-sunny line can cover at most 2 edge points, except for lines of the form x=1 or y=1. Wait, that's not true. Let's analyze the edge points E_n. They are all points with a=1 or b=1. For a vertical line x = d, it covers points (d,b) with b≤n+1-d. Among those, the edge points are (d,1) if d≥1 (b=1) and also (d,? if d=1 then all (1,b) are edge points. So for d>1, only (d,1) is an edge point. For d=1, it's all (1,b) which are n edge points. So the maximum edge points per vertical line is n (for d=1). Similarly, for horizontal line y = d, max edge points is n (for d=1). For slope -1 line x+y = e, the edge points are (1, e-1) and (e-1, 1) if those are within bounds. So that's at most 2. So the only lines that can cover many edge points are the axis-aligned lines with intercept 1. So if we have m non-sunny lines, we can include x=1 and y=1, covering 2n-1 edge points (but they overlap). That's two lines covering all edge points. So with m=2, we could cover all edge points. Then we would have k = n-2 sunny lines. That would be possible for n=4 (k=2) which is not allowed; we suspect k=2 impossible. So maybe the difficulty is not about edge points, but about the interior points that are not on the axes. The interior points form a triangle of size (n-2). To cover those, we need many non-sunny lines or many sunny lines. But we already argued that each sunny line can cover at most ⌊(n+1)/2⌋ - 1 interior points. That's about n/2 - 1. So with k sunny lines, they can cover at most k * (A-1) interior points. With m non-sunny lines, they can cover at most m * (n-2) interior points. The total interior points are (n-1)(n-2)/2. So we need:

k (A-1) + m (n-2) ≥ (n-1)(n-2)/2.

Now, m = n - k. Plug in:

k (A-1) + (n - k)(n-2) ≥ (n-1)(n-2)/2.

Simplify. Compute (n - k)(n-2) = n(n-2) - k(n-2). So inequality becomes:

k (A-1) + n(n-2) - k(n-2)) ≥ (n-1)(n-2)/2 → n(n-2) + k(A-1 - (n-2)) ≥ (n-1)(n-2)/2.

Now, A = ⌊(n+1)/2⌋. Compute A - 1 - (n-2) = A - n + 1. As before. So we get:

n(n-2) + k (A - n + 1) ≥ (n-1)(n-2)/2.

Now, compute RHS. For n=4, n(n-2)=4*2=8. (n-1)(n-2)/2=3*2/2=3. So inequality is 8 + k*(A - n + 1) ≥ 3. A - n + 1 = 2 - 4 + 1 = -1. So 8 - k ≥ 3 → k ≤ 5. So not restrictive. For n=5, n(n-2)=5*3=15. (n-1)(n-2)/2=4*3/2=6. A - n + 1 = 3 - 5 + 1 = -1. So 15 - k ≥ 6 → k ≤ 9. So again not restrictive.

Thus, interior points inequality doesn't forbid large k.

So we need a more sophisticated necessary condition. Let's try to understand the geometry of covering a triangular grid with lines. Perhaps we can use an approach based on the "sunny" condition: A line is sunny if it is not parallel to any of the x-axis, y-axis, or x+y=0. This means that if we consider the set of lines that are not sunny, they are exactly those that are horizontal, vertical, or have slope -1. So any line that is not sunny is one of three types. Now, suppose we have a covering set of n lines. If many of them are sunny, then we have few non-sunny lines. The non-sunny lines are limited in number. Could it be that the points in P_n that are not covered by the non-sunny lines must be covered by sunny lines, but sunny lines cannot cover certain patterns because they are not parallel to axes or diagonal? But they can be placed arbitrarily, so they can cover any set of points as long as they are collinear. However, the requirement is that each point must lie on at least one line. So if we have many sunny lines, we need to ensure that all points are covered. The question is: Is it possible to cover all points with, say, 3 sunny lines and 2 non-sunny lines for n=5? That would be 5 lines. We can try to design explicitly.

Let's attempt to construct for n=5 with k=3. So we need 3 sunny lines and 2 non-sunny lines. The total lines is 5. We need to cover 15 points. Let's try to use non-sunny lines that cover many points. The best coverage per line is n=5. So with 2 lines, we can cover up to 10 points. That leaves 5 points to cover with 3 sunny lines. But we also need to cover the points that are not covered by the non-sunny lines. Could we cover all 15 points with 2 non-sunny lines covering 10 points and 3 sunny lines covering the other 5? That seems plausible. But we must also ensure that the sunny lines are distinct and sunny. However, the problem is to determine all k such that there exist n lines. So we need to see if any construction exists. Our suspicion is that k ≤ ⌊(n-1)/2⌋. So for n=5, k ≤ 2. So we need to prove that k ≥ 3 is impossible. Let's try to prove that.

One idea: Consider the points with a = b (the diagonal). There are ⌊(n+1)/2⌋ such points. They are all on the line y = x. That line is sunny. So if we have many sunny lines, they could cover some of those points. But maybe we can argue that the points (1,1), (2,2), ..., (t,t) where t = ⌊(n+1)/2⌋ form a set that can be covered by a single sunny line (y=x). That's not helpful.

Another idea: Use a combinatorial double counting on triples (point, line, coordinate) or something like that. Perhaps we can consider the fact that any sunny line has slope s ≠ 0, ∞, -1. The points of P_n can be represented as (i,j) with i,j ≥ 1, i+j ≤ n+1. Now, suppose we have a covering with n lines. For each line L, let c_L be the number of points it covers. Sum c_L ≥ N_n. Now, we can also consider the number of points that are covered by at least two lines, but that doesn't matter.

Maybe we can prove a lower bound on the number of non-sunny lines needed to cover the points with a+b = n (the second layer). Let's compute the coverage of points in layer s by sunny lines. For a sunny line L with slope s ≠ 0, ∞, -1. It can intersect the line a+b = s at most once, because it's a line. So each sunny line covers at most 1 point in each layer. So the total number of points in layer s that can be covered by sunny lines is at most k. The non-sunny lines can cover many points in a layer. The layer s has s-1 points. The sum over layers of (s-1) is N_n. So if k is large, the sunny lines cover at most k points in each layer, but they could cover many layers. The non-sunny lines are few, so they must cover many points. The maximum number of points a non-sunny line can cover in a layer is at most 1 (for horizontal or vertical) except for slope -1 lines which can cover all points in a layer (if the layer is the hypotenuse). So to cover all points, we likely need many non-sunny lines to cover the interior layers. This is reminiscent of covering a triangular grid with lines where only lines of certain slopes are allowed to cover many points. The problem asks to determine all possible numbers of sunny lines. So we need to characterize the maximum possible k.

Let's attempt to prove that m ≥ ⌈(n+1)/2⌉ by contradiction. Assume m ≤ ⌊n/2⌋. Then k ≥ n - ⌊n/2⌋ = ⌈n/2⌉. That's a large number. We'll try to show that the total number of points that can be covered by the k sunny lines is insufficient, i.e., each sunny line can cover at most something like ⌊(n+1)/2⌋ points, but that's about n/2. If we have about n/2 sunny lines, they can cover at most (n/2)*(n/2) = n^2/4 points. That's less than N_n ≈ n^2/2. So the remaining points must be covered by non-sunny lines. But non-sunny lines are few, at most ⌊n/2⌋. However, each non-sunny line can cover at most n points. So the total capacity of non-sunny lines is at most ⌊n/2⌋ * n ≈ n^2/2. That's enough to cover the remaining points. So capacity is not the issue. The problem is that the sunny lines cannot cover many points, but they might be placed to cover the points that are not covered by non-sunny lines. The non-sunny lines can cover many points, but they are limited in number. The worst-case scenario for covering is when the non-sunny lines are placed to cover as many points as possible, but we need to cover all. So if we have only a few non-sunny lines, they might be forced to cover many points, but they cannot cover all points because of some structural constraints. Let's examine the construction for n=5 with k=3. That used 3 sunny lines covering interior points. The interior points were covered by three sunny lines that are pairwise intersecting and each passes through two interior points. That seems to be a covering of the interior triangle (size 3) with 3 lines. That's possible because the interior triangle (with a,b≥2) is a triangle of side length 3 (in terms of coordinates). The maximum number of points a sunny line can cover in that triangle is ⌊(n+1)/2⌋ - 1 = 2. So with 3 sunny lines, we can cover up to 6 points, which is exactly the number of interior points. Let's compute the maximum possible k for n=5 using a similar reasoning. The interior points are 6. The maximum points per sunny line is 2. So if we have k sunny lines, they can cover at most 2k interior points. The non-sunny lines can cover at most (n - k) * (n-2) interior points. The total interior points must be covered. So we need:

2k + (n - k)*(n-2) ≥ |I_n| = (n-1)(n-2)/2.

For n=5, that's 2k + (5 - k)*3 ≥ 6 → 2k + 15 - 3k ≥ 6 → 15 - k ≥ 6 → k ≤ 9. That's not restrictive. So that doesn't bound k.

The real constraint seems to be about covering the "edges" of the triangle (the points on the axes) with sunny lines, given that sunny lines can cover at most 2 such points each. But we also have non-sunny lines. The edge points are 2n-1. The sunny lines can cover at most 2k edge points. The limitation is that some points might be forced to be covered by lines that are not sunny, but we only have sunny lines. Now, we need to determine the maximum possible k for general n. The idea is: Suppose we have n lines, exactly k sunny, m = n - k non-sunny. The non-sunny lines are of types H,V,D. Actually we need to derive a lower bound on m based on the fact that each sunny line can cover at most some number of points from some subset, and we need to cover all points. The subset we used in the n=5 case was the complement of a specific non-sunny line. But in general, we don't know which line is non-sunny. The bound should be derived from the covering requirement regardless of which lines are chosen. The argument that with m non-sunny lines, the uncovered set (points not on any non-sunny line) must be covered by sunny lines. The size of that uncovered set depends on the placement of the non-sunny lines. To get a necessary condition on m, we can consider the worst-case scenario for covering: we want to minimize the number of non-sunny lines needed to cover all points. The problem asks: for which k does there exist a configuration? The argument we used to rule out k=4 was that with m=1 (1 non-sunny), the uncovered set (10 points) cannot be covered by 4 sunny lines because each sunny line can cover at most 2 points of that set. But is it always true that each sunny line can cover at most 2 points of any subset? That depends on the subset. In the n=5 case, we considered the complement of x=1, which is a set of 10 points. We observed that any sunny line can contain at most 2 of those points. That was because the set had a structure (like many points on a vertical line). The argument using the complement of a non-sunny line might be used to bound m. But we need a general bound. Let's analyze the necessary condition.

We have n lines. Consider the points that lie on the axes (a=1 or b=1). There are 2n-1 such points. The sunny lines can cover at most 2 of them each (since a sunny line can intersect each axis at most once). So the total number of axis points that can be covered by sunny lines is at most 2k. The remaining axis points must be covered by non-sunny lines. A non-sunny line can cover many axis points. For example, a vertical line x=1 covers all axis points with a=1, which are n points. Similarly, a horizontal line y=1 covers n points. A slope -1 line covers at most 2 axis points. So to cover all axis points, we need enough non-sunny lines to cover the axis points that are not covered by sunny lines. That gives a constraint: 2n-1 ≤ 2k + (axis coverage from non-sunny lines). But non-sunny lines can be chosen to cover axis points efficiently. The number of axis points covered by m non-sunny lines is at most something like 2m? Actually if we take x=1 and y=1, that's 2 lines covering all axis points. That's m=2. So with m=2, we can cover all axis points. So that doesn't bound k.

Now, consider the points with a,b ≥ 2 (interior). There are I_n = (n-1)(n-2)/2. The sunny lines can cover at most B = ⌊(n+1)/2⌋ - 1 interior points each. The non-sunny lines can cover at most (n-2) interior points each (if they are axis-aligned with intercept 2). So the total interior coverage from all lines must be at least I_n. That's a necessary condition. But as we saw, for n=5, this inequality is satisfied for many k. So not restrictive.

The key observation in the n=5 case that ruled out k=4 was that with m=1, the uncovered set S (complement of that non-sunny line) had size 10, and any sunny line can cover at most 2 points of S. Thus, with 4 sunny lines, we could cover at most 8 points, insufficient. That's a general argument. But does it hold for all n? For a given n, if we have m non-sunny lines, we can consider the complement of the union of those lines. That's a set of points that are not on any non-sunny line. To get a lower bound on m, we need to show that if m is small, then no matter how we place the non-sunny lines, the complement will be too large to be covered by the remaining sunny lines (since each sunny line can cover at most some number of points from that complement). However, the complement could be small if the non-sunny lines are placed cleverly. So we need to find a lower bound on m such that for any covering with m non-sunny lines, the complement's size is ≤ (n - m) * (max points per sunny line). But that's not a necessary condition because we can choose the non-sunny lines to minimize the complement size. The problem is to determine all k for which there exists some set of lines. So we can try to construct a covering with a certain k, but to prove an upper bound on k, we need to show that if k is larger than some threshold, then any covering must have m ≥ something. That is, we need to prove that for any covering, m ≥ f(n). That's a necessary condition. So we need to find a lower bound on m that holds for any covering. Then k ≤ n - m.

Thus, we need to find a combinatorial invariant that forces many non-sunny lines, regardless of which lines are chosen. The example with n=5 shows that m can be as low as 2, so f(n) ≤ 2 for n=5. For n=4, m can be 3, so f(n) ≤ 3. So the bound is not simply a function like floor(n/2) or ceil(n/2). Let's compute the possible m for n=3,4,5:

n=3: we have a covering with m=2 (k=1) possible. So m_min = 2. (k=2 impossible)

In I_n, which corresponds to a'≥1,b'≥1 with a'+b'≤5, the points (a',b') are (2,2) → (a=3,b=3) in I_n? Actually a' = a-1, b' = b-1. So if a'=2,b'=2, then a=3,b=3. That's in I_n. So the line y = x in P_5 (original) corresponds to a'=a-1,b'=b-1 → (a',b') = (t-1,t-1) if a=b=t. So that's points with a'=t-1,b'=t-1. For t=2 → a'=1,b'=1 → that's (a=2,b=2) in I_n. For t=3 → a'=2,b'=2 → (a=3,b=3) in I_n. For t=4 → a'=3,b'=3 → (a=4,b=4) but a+b=8 >7, not in I_n. So indeed, the line y=x contains only (2,2) and (3,3) from I_n, which are 2 points. So the maximum sunny points in I_n is not simply ⌊n/2⌋; it's the maximum number of points from I_n that can lie on a sunny line. Since I_n is a translation of P_{n-1} by (1,1). So the maximum number of points in I_n on a line of slope s is the maximum number of integer points in the translated triangle on that line. For slope 1, the line y = x + c. The points in I_n satisfy a≥2,b≥2,a+b≤n+1. For c = 0, the points are (t,t) with t≥2 and 2t ≤ n+1 → t ≤ floor((n+1)/2). So the number is max(0, floor((n+1)/2) - 1). For n=6, that's floor(7/2)=3 → 3-1=2. So indeed, the maximum is 2. For slope 1/2, we might get up to something. So the bound on sunny points per line in I_n is floor((n+1)/2) - 1, which is about n/2 - 1. For n=5, that's 2; for n=6, that's 2; for n=7, that's 3? floor((8)/2)=4 → 3. So for n=7, I_n size = (6*5)/2 = 15, max sunny points per line could be 3. So with m=2, we would need to cover I_n with (n - m) = 5 sunny lines. That's 5 lines * 3 = 15, which could be enough. So we need to determine the general bound.

Let's try to derive a necessary condition for covering. Suppose we have a covering with m non-sunny lines and k sunny lines. Consider the set of points with a,b ≥ 2. This set has size I_n = (n-1)(n-2)/2. These points must be covered by the lines. The non-sunny lines can cover some of them. The sunny lines can cover some. The maximum number of points from I_n that a sunny line can cover is something like B = ⌊(n+1)/2⌋ - 1. Let's denote L = ⌊(n+1)/2⌋ - 1. For n even, n=2t, L = t - 1. For n odd, n=2t+1, L = t.

Now, each non-sunny line can cover at most n-2 points from I_n (since axis-aligned lines with intercept 2 cover n-2 points). But is that always the maximum? A non-sunny line of type H (horizontal) y = c covers points with b = c. For it to cover points in I_n, we need c ≥ 2. Then the number of points in I_n on that line is at most (n+1 - c) - 2 + 1 = n - c. The maximum occurs at c = 2, giving n-2. Similarly, vertical lines x = d give n-2 at d=2. Slope -1 lines D_e: points with a+b = e, e ≥ 4. The number of points in I_n on D_e is at most (e-3) (since a from 2 to e-2). The maximum occurs at e = n+1, giving (n+1)-3 = n-2. So indeed, any non-sunny line can cover at most n-2 points from I_n. So we have:

|I_n| ≤ m * (n-2) + k * L.

Now, |I_n| = (n-1)(n-2)/2. So:

(n-1)(n-2)/2 ≤ m (n-2) + k L.

Plug m = n - k:

(n-1)(n-2)/2 ≤ (n - k)(n-2) + k L.

Compute RHS = (n-2)n - k(n-2) + k L = n(n-2) + k (L - n + 2).

Thus:

k (L - n + 2) ≥ (n-1)(n-2)/2 - n(n-2).

Compute RHS:

n(n-2) = n^2 - 2n.
(n-1)(n-2)/2 = (n^2 - 3n + 2)/2.
Difference = (n^2 - 3n + 2)/2 - (n^2 - 2n) = (n^2 - 3n + 2 - 2n^2 + 4n)/2 = (-n^2 + n + 2)/2.

So:

k (L - n + 2) ≥ (-n^2 + n + 2)/2.

Now, L - n + 2 = (⌊(n+1)/2⌋ - 1) - n + 2 = ⌊(n+1)/2⌋ - n + 1.

Compute:

If n is even, n = 2t: ⌊(2t+1)/2⌋ = t, so L - n + 2 = t - 2t + 1 = -t + 1 = 1 - t. This is negative for t ≥ 2. For t=2 (n=4), it's 1-2 = -1. For t=3 (n=6), it's 1-3 = -2. For t=1 (n=2) not considered.

If n is odd, n = 2t+1: ⌊(2t+2)/2⌋ = t+1, so L = t, then L - n + 2 = t - (2t+1) + 2 = t - 2t - 1 + 2 = -t + 1 = 1 - t. For t=1 (n=3), it's 0. For t=2 (n=5), it's -1. So L - n + 2 is non-positive, and zero only for n=3. So the coefficient is negative for n ≥ 4. That means the inequality becomes:

k * (negative) ≥ (negative). Dividing by the negative flips inequality, giving:

k ≤ \frac{(-n^2 + n + 2)/2}{L - n + 2}.

But L - n + 2 is negative, so the fraction is something. Let's compute it for n=5: L = 2, L - n + 2 = 2 - 5 + 2 = -1. RHS numerator = (-25 + 5 + 2)/2 = (-18)/2 = -9. Then k ≤ (-9)/(-1) = 9. So trivial. So that inequality does not bound k.

Thus, the interior points inequality is too weak. Compute:

If n is even, n = 2t: ⌊(2t+1)/2⌋ = t, so L - n + 2 = t - 2t + 1 = -t + 1 = 1 - t. For t≥2, this is negative. For n=4, t=2 → -1; for n=6, t=3 → -2; etc.

If n is odd, n = 2t+1: ⌊(2t+2)/2⌋ = t+1, so L - n + 2 = (t+1) - (2t+1) + 2 = t+1 - 2t -1 + 2 = -t + 2 = 2 - t. For t=1 (n=3), this is 1; for t=2 (n=5), it's 0; for t=3 (n=7), it's -1; etc.

Thus, the coefficient (L - n + 2) is non-positive for n≥4. For n=3, it's positive. So for n≥4, we have:

k * (negative) ≥ (negative). Dividing by the negative flips inequality, giving:

We need to solve a combinatorial geometry / covering problem. Let me restate:

We have n >= 3 given integer. Determine all nonnegative integers k such that there exist n distinct lines in the plane satisfying:
- For all positive integers a and b with a+b <= n+1, the point (a,b) lies on at least one of the lines.
- Exactly k of the n lines are sunny.

Definition: A line is sunny if it is not parallel to any of the x-axis, the y-axis, or the line x+y=0. So sunny lines are those that are not horizontal, not vertical, and not with slope -1 (since x+y=0 => y = -x, slope -1). Actually "parallel to the line x+y=0" means lines of slope -1, because they are parallel to that line. So sunny lines are those that are not horizontal, not vertical, and not slope -1. But note that a line could be parallel to x-axis (horizontal), or y-axis (vertical), or the line x+y=0 (slope -1). If it is parallel to any of those, it is not sunny. So sunny lines must have slopes that are not 0, not infinite, and not -1. Also they are not parallel to those lines, so they are not horizontal, not vertical, and not with slope -1. So sunny lines are lines with slope ≠ 0, ≠ ∞, ≠ -1.

We need to find all k for which there exists a set of n distinct lines (could be any lines) such that the set of points (a,b) with a,b positive integers and a+b <= n+1 is covered (each such point lies on at least one line). And exactly k of those lines are sunny.

We need to determine all possible k. Since n >= 3, we need to see constraints.

First, let's understand the coverage requirement. Points with positive integer coordinates (a,b) (a,b >=1) and a+b <= n+1. So we have a triangular grid of points. The number of such points is sum_{s=2}^{n+1} (s-1) = 1+2+...+n = n(n+1)/2. Actually a,b>=1, a+b <= n+1. For a+b = t, where t ranges from 2 to n+1, number of pairs is (t-1). Sum_{t=2}^{n+1} (t-1) = sum_{i=1}^{n} i = n(n+1)/2. So there are N = n(n+1)/2 points to cover.

We have n lines. Each line can cover many points. The condition is that each point must lie on at least one line. We can choose lines arbitrarily, but they must be distinct. Also we want exactly k of them to be sunny. So we need to see for which k such a configuration exists.

This is reminiscent of covering points with lines, like "determine the minimal number of lines needed to cover a set of points" or "max number of sunny lines possible". But here we have a fixed number n of lines, and we need to cover all those points. So we need to see if there is a covering by n lines with exactly k sunny lines. For some k it might be impossible, for others possible. Determine all nonnegative integers k (k ≤ n) for which such a set exists.

We should analyze constraints. The lines are not required to be in general position, they can intersect. Since we have n lines, and we need to cover N points. No, two points determine a line, so if a line passes through two points, that line is uniquely determined. Let's analyze more systematically.

Let n ≥ 3 be given. Let P_n = {(a,b) ∈ ℕ^2 : a,b ≥ 1, a+b ≤ n+1}. We have |P_n| = n(n+1)/2.

We have n lines L_1,...,L_n distinct. Let S be the set of sunny lines among them. |S| = k.

We need to cover P_n. The lines that are not sunny (i.e., not sunny) are those that are parallel to x-axis, y-axis, or x+y=0. They are non-sunny. Let's denote N = n - k (non-sunny lines). They are of types: horizontal (H), vertical (V), slope -1 (D). (We could also have lines that are parallel to these but not exactly those, but they are all lines with those slopes.)

We need to find all k for which there exists a covering of P_n by n lines with exactly k sunny lines. That's the problem.

Idea: We can try to construct configurations with many sunny lines. For instance, we could take all lines to be sunny, i.e., k = n. But is that possible? For n=3, we saw that k=3 is impossible. Now, we need to prove that lower bound on m. How to prove that? Let's attempt.

We have n lines. Let’s denote the set of non-sunny lines as N (size m). They are lines with slopes 0, ∞, or -1. For each such line, we can consider the points it covers. If this fails, then any covering must have more non-sunny lines. So we can get a lower bound on m. But is this inequality always true for m small? Let's test with n=4. Then ⌊(n+1)/2⌋ = ⌊5/2⌋ = 2.
