Let's think about covering with lines. The problem is reminiscent of "cover all points in a triangular grid with lines, where some lines are forbidden". Usually, such problems ask to find the minimal number of lines needed. But here we have exactly n lines, and we want to determine possible numbers of sunny lines. The bound might come from considering the "inversions" or "pairings" of points with respect to lines. Another idea: Use a double counting argument with pairs (point, line) where the point lies on the line. For each point, it must be covered by at least one line. So total incidences (point-line pairs) is at least N_n. On the other hand, each line can cover at most some number of points. For non-sunny lines, we can bound the maximum number of points they can cover by n (since they are of types H,V,D). For sunny lines, we can bound the maximum number of points they can cover by ⌊(n+1)/2⌋. So if we have m non-sunny lines and k sunny lines, then the total incidences is at most m * n + k * ⌊(n+1)/2⌋. Since we need at least N_n incidences, we have:

m * n + k * ⌊(n+1)/2⌋ ≥ N_n = n(n+1)/2.

But note that m = n - k. So substitute: (n - k) * n + k * ⌊(n+1)/2⌋ ≥ n(n+1)/2.

Simplify: n^2 - nk + k * ⌊(n+1)/2⌋ ≥ n(n+1)/2.

We want to find the maximum k such that this inequality holds. If it fails, then any covering would be impossible. However, the inequality is a necessary condition, but is it also sufficient for existence? Not necessarily, but if we can prove that if k is too large, then the inequality cannot hold, then such k are impossible. So we can get an upper bound on k.

Let's compute:

(n - k) n + k ⌊(n+1)/2⌋ = n^2 - nk + k ⌊(n+1)/2⌋ = n^2 + k (⌊(n+1)/2⌋ - n).

So we need:

n^2 + k (⌊(n+1)/2⌋ - n) ≥ n(n+1)/2.

Now, ⌊(n+1)/2⌋ is roughly n/2. So ⌊(n+1)/2⌋ - n is about -n/2. So the left side is n^2 minus about (n/2)k. The right side is about n^2/2. So the inequality becomes:

n^2 - (something) k ≥ n^2/2 → (something) k ≤ n^2/2 → k ≤ (n^2/2) / (something). But we need to solve precisely.

Let’s denote:

A = ⌊(n+1)/2⌋. Then inequality:

n^2 - nk + k A ≥ n(n+1)/2.

=> n^2 + k(A - n) ≥ n(n+1)/2.

=> k(A - n) ≥ n(n+1)/2 - n^2 = (n^2 + n - 2n^2)/2 = (-n^2 + n)/2 = n(1 - n)/2 = -n(n-1)/2.

Since A - n is negative. Multiply both sides by -1 (reversing inequality):

k (n - A) ≤ n(n-1)/2.

Thus:

k ≤ \frac{n(n-1)}{2(n - A)}.

Now, compute n - A. A = ⌊(n+1)/2⌋.

We need to compute n - ⌊(n+1)/2⌋.

Case n even: n = 2t. Then n+1 = 2t+1, ⌊(2t+1)/2⌋ = t. So A = t. Then n - A = 2t - t = t. So n - A = t = n/2.

Thus k ≤ \frac{n(n-1)}{2 * (n/2)} = \frac{n(n-1)}{n} = n-1.

So we get k ≤ n-1, which is trivial (k ≤ n). That doesn't give a strong bound. For n odd: n = 2t+1. Then n+1 = 2t+2, ⌊(2t+2)/2⌋ = t+1. So A = t+1. Then n - A = (2t+1) - (t+1) = t. So n - A = t = (n-1)/2. Then k ≤ \frac{n(n-1)}{2 * ((n-1)/2)} = \frac{n(n-1)}{n-1} = n. So again trivial. So this inequality gives no useful bound. That's because we used the maximum number of points a sunny line can cover, but we also used that non-sunny lines can cover up to n points. However, the bound m * n + k * A is an upper bound on incidences, but we need a lower bound. So if the upper bound is less than N_n, then impossible. That's what we did. But we got trivial bound because the upper bound is large enough. So that doesn't restrict k.

Thus we need a different approach. Perhaps we need to consider the "sunny" condition and prove that the number of sunny lines cannot exceed something like ⌊(n-1)/2⌋. But the inequality we derived was an upper bound on incidences, not a lower bound. To get a contradiction, we need to show that if k is large, then the total incidences (point-line pairs) from the set of lines must be at least N_n, but the maximum possible incidences from those lines is less than N_n. So we need to show that if k > ⌊(n-1)/2⌋, then covering cannot be achieved. So we need to prove that any covering must have m ≥ ⌈(n+1)/2⌉. That's a lower bound on m. So we need to derive that m cannot be too small. That is, if we have too few non-sunny lines, then there are too many points that cannot be covered by the remaining sunny lines (or that the sunny lines cannot cover all points because of some structural constraints). This seems plausible. Let's attempt to prove that.

We have n lines. Let’s denote the set of sunny lines as S (size k), non-sunny as N (size m). We need to cover P_n. Consider the points with a+b = n+1 (hypotenuse). There are n points. We need to find a more refined necessary condition that forces many non-sunny lines. Let's think about the structure of covering. The points are in a right triangle. A line can cover many points only if it is axis-aligned or slope -1. Those are non-sunny. Sunny lines are "other" lines. The difficult points are those that cannot be covered by a sunny line in a structured way? Actually any point can be covered by a sunny line if we place that line appropriately. But we have limited lines. The problem is to determine all k such that there exists some set of n lines (distinct) with exactly k sunny covering all points. So we can try to construct for each k up to some bound. The difficulty is to find a construction for k = ⌊(n-1)/2⌋. That seems plausible. Let's attempt to construct.

We need n lines. Suppose we take ⌊(n-1)/2⌋ sunny lines, and the rest are non-sunny. The non-sunny lines will be of types that cover many points. For n even, say n = 2t. Actually for n even, n = 2t, t = n/2, then non-sunny lines = n - (t-1) = 2t - t + 1 = t+1. For n odd, n = 2t+1, then non-sunny = (2t+1) - t = t+1. That is equivalent to m = ⌈(n+1)/2⌉? Let's test: n=3 → ⌈4/2⌉=2, yes; n=4 → ⌈5/2⌉=3, yes; n=5 → ⌈6/2⌉=3, yes. So m = ⌈(n+1)/2⌉. So the lower bound we suspect is m ≥ ⌈(n+1)/2⌉. That is exactly that the minimum number of non-sunny lines needed is at least that. So we need to prove that any covering of P_n by n lines must have at least ⌈(n+1)/2⌉ non-sunny lines. That's a necessary condition. Then the maximum possible k is n - ⌈(n+1)/2⌉ = ⌊(n-1)/2⌋.

Thus, the problem reduces to proving that m ≥ ⌈(n+1)/2⌉. Then we also need to demonstrate that for any k ≤ ⌊(n-1)/2⌋, there exists a configuration with exactly k sunny lines. That is, all such k are achievable. So we need to provide constructions.

Now, how to prove m ≥ ⌈(n+1)/2⌉? Let's attempt.

We have n lines. Let's denote the set of non-sunny lines as N (size m). They are lines with slopes 0, ∞, or -1. For each such line, we can consider the set of points it covers. But perhaps we can use a double counting argument on the "inversions" or "pairings" of points with respect to lines. Another approach: Consider the points with a+b = n+1 (hypotenuse). There are n points. Suppose we have k sunny lines. Each sunny line can cover at most 1 of these points. So the sunny lines cover at most k points on the hypotenuse. The remaining n - k points must be covered by non-sunny lines. That's fine. But maybe we can argue that the non-sunny lines must cover all points on the hypotenuse, and that each non-sunny line can cover at most n points, but we need to cover n points, so that doesn't bound m. However, we can also consider points with a+b = n (the next layer). There are n-1 points. Sunny lines can cover at most 1 each. So if we have many sunny lines, they might cover many points across layers, but we need to bound the total coverage of sunny lines.

Maybe we can use an extremal principle: Among all covering sets of n lines, consider one with minimum number of non-sunny lines. Let m be that minimum. Show that m ≥ ⌈(n+1)/2⌉. So assume that there exists a covering with m non-sunny lines. We'll derive a contradiction if m < ⌈(n+1)/2⌉. That is, if m ≤ ⌈(n+1)/2⌉ - 1. Then k = n - m ≥ n - (⌈(n+1)/2⌉ - 1) = ⌊(n-1)/2⌋ + 1? Actually we want to prove that k ≤ ⌊(n-1)/2⌋, i.e., m ≥ ⌈(n+1)/2⌉. So we need to prove that if m ≤ ⌈(n+1)/2⌉ - 1, then covering is impossible. That's the contrapositive. So we assume m is small (few non-sunny lines). Then we try to show that the total number of points that can be covered by those lines plus the sunny lines (which are many) is insufficient. But we already saw that with m small, the capacity of sunny lines (max points per line) is about n/2, and we have many sunny lines. The sum of capacities could still be large. But we need to consider that the sunny lines cannot cover arbitrary points; they are limited in number. However, we can choose them. The question is: given a small m, can we arrange m non-sunny lines and (n - m) sunny lines such that together they cover all points? That's a covering problem. The lower bound we want to prove is that such a covering cannot exist. So we need a proof that any covering of P_n by n lines must contain at least ⌈(n+1)/2⌉ lines that are either horizontal, vertical, or slope -1. This is a combinatorial geometry statement.

We can attempt to prove it by considering the points (a,b) with a,b ≥ 2, i.e., the "interior" triangle. There are (n-1)(n-2)/2 such points. Suppose we have only m non-sunny lines. Then the number of sunny lines is n - m. We want to see if it's possible that the sunny lines cover all those interior points. But as we saw, each sunny line can cover at most ⌊(n+1)/2⌋ points. The maximum total points from sunny lines is (n - m) * ⌊(n+1)/2⌋. So we need:

(n - m) * ⌊(n+1)/2⌋ ≥ (n-1)(n-2)/2.

If this inequality fails, then some interior points cannot be covered by sunny lines alone, and they must be covered by non-sunny lines. However, the non-sunny lines also can cover interior points. But we have only m non-sunny lines, which can cover at most m * n points (but they might cover interior points). But we need to guarantee that all points are covered. So if m is small, then the total capacity of non-sunny lines (max distinct points they can cover) is at most m * n. However, they might not be able to cover many interior points because the interior points are bounded. But we can try to get a contradiction by considering that the total number of points (N_n) is sum over layers. The capacity of sunny lines is (n - m) * A. The capacity of non-sunny lines is m * n. So total capacity is m n + (n - m) A. Since A ≈ n/2, this total is roughly m n + (n - m) n/2 = (n^2 + m n)/2? Actually compute: m n + (n - m) A. A = floor((n+1)/2). For n large, this is about n/2. So total capacity ≈ m n + (n - m) n/2 = (m n) + (n^2/2 - m n/2) = n^2/2 + (m n)/2. That's larger than N_n = n^2/2 + n/2. So the capacity is larger than needed. So that doesn't force m to be large.

Thus, we need a different kind of invariant.

Maybe we can consider the "sunny" condition and prove that each non-sunny line can cover at most n points, but also that any sunny line can cover at most something like ⌊(n+1)/2⌋ points. However, the total points covered by all lines is at least N_n. So we have:

∑_{L ∈ L} |L ∩ P_n| ≥ N_n. (1)

Now, each line L has |L ∩ P_n| ≤ f(L), where f(L) depends on the slope. For non-sunny lines, f(L) ≤ n. For sunny lines, f(L) ≤ ⌊(n+1)/2⌋. So

∑_{L ∈ L} |L ∩ P_n| ≤ m n + k ⌊(n+1)/2⌋.

Thus we have:

m n + k ⌊(n+1)/2⌋ ≥ N_n.

But this is an upper bound on left side? Actually the sum of |L ∩ P_n| is at most something. If that something is less than N_n, then impossible. So we need to check if for small m (i.e., large k), this inequality could fail. But as we saw, it's satisfied for all k, so not a contradiction. However, note that the inequality is "≤" for the sum, not "≥". So we need to derive a lower bound on the sum of coverage from the lines. But we have a lower bound (N_n) on the sum (since each point must be covered by at least one line). So we have:

N_n ≤ ∑_{L} |L ∩ P_n| ≤ m n + k A.

Thus N_n ≤ m n + k A. That's a necessary condition. That's what we wrote. So if N_n > m n + k A, then impossible. But we computed N_n = n(n+1)/2. m n + k A = (n - k) n + k A = n^2 - nk + k A = n^2 + k(A - n). Since A - n is negative, this is n^2 - (n - A) k. The inequality N_n ≤ n^2 - (n - A) k becomes:

n(n+1)/2 ≤ n^2 - (n - A) k => (n - A) k ≤ n^2 - n(n+1)/2 = (2n^2 - n^2 - n)/2 = (n^2 - n)/2 = n(n-1)/2.

Thus k ≤ \frac{n(n-1)}{2(n - A)}. This gave trivial bound for both n even and odd. So that doesn't help. The bound is weak because the capacity of non-sunny lines (n) is large, and capacity of sunny lines is about n/2. So even if k is large (like n-1), the total capacity is still large. So this inequality doesn't rule out large k. So we need a stronger bound. The problem asks to determine all k such that there exist n lines. So we need to find which k are achievable. Perhaps we can prove that the maximum possible k is something like ⌊(n-1)/2⌋. That is an upper bound. Then we need to construct for all k up to that bound. So we need to prove that if k > ⌊(n-1)/2⌋, then impossible. That is, any covering must have at most ⌊(n-1)/2⌋ sunny lines, i.e., at least ⌈(n+1)/2⌉ non-sunny lines. So we need to prove that m ≥ ⌈(n+1)/2⌉. So we need a proof that no covering can have m less than that.

Let's try to find a proof. Consider the points with a+b = n+1 (hypotenuse). There are n points. Suppose we have m non-sunny lines. How many of these hypotenuse points can be covered by non-sunny lines? Possibly all of them if we use a line with slope -1 that goes through all those points. That's one line. So m could be 1. So that doesn't give a bound.

But perhaps we need to consider the "star" of points with a+b = n (the layer just inside). There are n-1 points. A sunny line can cover at most 1 of those. So if we have many sunny lines, they cover many points in that layer. However, we have to cover all points. The points in the interior are many. The total number of points is about n^2/2. If we have few non-sunny lines, then the sunny lines must cover almost all points. But each sunny line covers at most about n/2 points, so we would need at least about n sunny lines to cover all points. That's not a contradiction. Let's compute: Suppose we have k sunny lines. The maximum points they can cover is k * A. The non-sunny lines can cover at most m * n. But we need to cover N_n points. So we need k * A + m * n ≥ N_n. Since m = n - k, this becomes (n - k) n + k A ≥ N_n. That's the same inequality. As we computed, this inequality holds for all k, but we want to prove that if k is too large, then covering is impossible. So the inequality must be satisfied, but if it's satisfied, it's not a contradiction. So the inequality alone does not forbid large k. So we need something else. Perhaps we can prove that each sunny line can cover at most ⌊(n+1)/2⌋ points, but also that each non-sunny line can cover at most something, but the sum of capacities might exceed N_n, but the actual coverage might be limited because lines are distinct and cannot be arranged to cover all points without overlapping? But we can choose them. So we need to find a structural impossibility.

Maybe we can consider the set of points (a,b) where a and b are both at least 2. That's a triangle. The lines that are not sunny are the only ones that can cover points in this triangle in a "structured" way. But maybe we can prove that any sunny line can intersect that triangle in at most something like ⌊(n-1)/2⌋ points? Actually the triangle of interior points (a≥2,b≥2) has size (n-1)(n-2)/2. A line with slope 1 (y=x) passes through points where a=b. For a≥2,b≥2, a=b can be up to floor((n+1)/2). That's about n/2. So that's not a problem. But perhaps we can prove that each sunny line can cover at most ⌊n/2⌋ points overall, but we already have that. So maybe the bound is derived from considering that the non-sunny lines must be distinct and there are n lines total. If we have many sunny lines, then the non-sunny lines are few. But the non-sunny lines are limited in number, and they cannot cover all points because the sunny lines also cover points. However, we need to find a contradiction based on the fact that some points cannot be covered by any sunny line if the number of sunny lines is too high? That seems unlikely.

Let's think about the possibility that k = n-1 is impossible. For n=4, k=3 is impossible. For n=5, k=4 is impossible. That's a stronger condition. The bound we derived (k ≤ n-1) is trivial, but we want to show that k ≤ ⌊(n-1)/2⌋. That's much smaller. So there must be a more subtle argument. Let's test with n=4, we have k=1 possible, k=0 possible. k=2 impossible. That's consistent with ⌊(n-1)/2⌋=1. So indeed, k cannot be 2. For n=5, ⌊(n-1)/2⌋=2, so k cannot be 3 or 4. So we need to prove that k ≤ 2. That seems plausible. But why is k=3 impossible? Let's attempt a construction for n=5 with k=3, i.e., 3 sunny lines and 2 non-sunny lines. Total lines 5. So we need to show that with 2 non-sunny lines, it's impossible to cover all 15 points. That seems plausible because the non-sunny lines are inefficient at covering interior points. But we need a rigorous proof.

Maybe the key is to consider the points with a+b = n+1 (hypotenuse) and also points with a+b = n. The non-sunny lines can cover many points on those layers, but sunny lines can cover at most 1 each. However, we have a limited number of sunny lines. The total number of points on the hypotenuse is n. Let's attempt to prove a lower bound on the number of non-sunny lines needed to cover the points with a,b≥2.

Actually we can compute the number of points on L ∩ P_n with a,b ≥ 2. For L: y = x (c=0). Then points are (t,t) with 2t ≤ n+1 ⇒ t ≤ ⌊(n+1)/2⌋. Among those, t≥2 gives t from 2 to ⌊(n+1)/2⌋. Let’s define:

- Total points N_n.
- Interior points I_n = {(a,b) : a,b ≥ 2, a+b ≤ n+1}. Size |I_n| = (n-1)(n-2)/2.

Now, each line L (non-sunny or sunny) can cover at most some number of interior points. But we can bound the maximum interior points per line.

For a non-sunny line:
- Horizontal y = c. For points with a,b≥2, we need b = c ≥ 2. Also a ≥ 2 and a + c ≤ n+1 ⇒ a ≤ n+1 - c. So the number of interior points on H_c is at most (n+1 - c) - 2 + 1 = n - c. Actually a from 2 to n+1 - c inclusive, count = (n+1 - c) - 2 + 1 = n - c. So H_c covers at most n - c interior points. The maximum occurs at c=2, giving n-2 interior points. For c=1, no interior points because b=1, b≥2 fails. So non-sunny horizontal lines can cover at most n-2 interior points. Similarly, vertical lines x = d cover at most n - d interior points (with d≥2). So max at d=2 gives n-2. For slope -1 lines D_e: points with a+b = e. For interior points, we need a,b ≥ 2 ⇒ e ≥ 4. Also a,b ≤ e-2, so number of interior points on D_e is (e-3) (since a from 2 to e-2, count = (e-2)-2+1 = e-3). The maximum occurs at e = n+1, giving (n+1)-3 = n-2 interior points. So indeed, any non-sunny line can cover at most n-2 interior points. (But also, if we have a line like x=1 or y=1, they cover no interior points.) So non-sunny lines are efficient at covering interior points, but each covers at most n-2.

For a sunny line, we need to bound the maximum number of interior points it can contain. As we saw, a sunny line with slope 1 (y=x) covers (⌊(n+1)/2⌋ - 1) interior points. That's about n/2 - 1. For a sunny line with slope 2, it covers fewer. So the maximum interior points per sunny line is achieved by slope 1. So max interior points per sunny line is ⌊(n+1)/2⌋ - 1 (if n+1 is even, that's (n+1)/2 - 1 = (n-1)/2; if n+1 is odd, that's (n)/2 - 1? Actually compute: n=5 → n+1=6 → ⌊6/2⌋=3 → interior points = 3-1=2. That's (n-1)/2? (5-1)/2=2. So indeed, for odd n, (n-1)/2. For n=4, n+1=5 → ⌊5/2⌋=2 → interior points = 2-1=1. That's (n-2)/2? Actually (4-2)/2=1. So it's floor((n-1)/2) maybe. Let's compute precisely:

Let A = ⌊(n+1)/2⌋. Then number of points on y=x with a=b and a+b ≤ n+1 is A (since a ≤ A). Among those, a≥2 gives max(0, A-1). So interior points from y=x is max(0, A-1). That's ⌊(n+1)/2⌋ - 1. So that's about n/2 - 1. For n even, n=2t, then A = t, interior = t-1 = n/2 - 1. For n odd, n=2t+1, A = t+1, interior = t = (n-1)/2. So it's roughly n/2 - 1.

Now, the total number of interior points is (n-1)(n-2)/2. If we have m non-sunny lines, they can cover at most m * (n-2) interior points. The sunny lines (k of them) can cover at most k * (A-1) interior points. So we have:

m * (n-2) + k * (A-1) ≥ |I_n| = (n-1)(n-2)/2.   (1)

Since m = n - k. Substitute:

(n - k) (n-2) + k (A-1) ≥ (n-1)(n-2)/2.

Now, compute (n - k)(n-2) = n(n-2) - k(n-2). So left side = n(n-2) - k(n-2) + k(A-1) = n(n-2) + k( (A-1) - (n-2) ) = n(n-2) + k( A - n + 1 ).

Now, A - n is negative. So we have:

n(n-2) + k (A - n + 1) ≥ (n-1)(n-2)/2.

We can attempt to see whether this inequality forces a lower bound on m, i.e., an upper bound on k. Solve for k.

Compute (n-1)(n-2)/2 = (n^2 - 3n + 2)/2.

n(n-2) = n^2 - 2n.

So inequality:

n^2 - 2n + k (A - n + 1) ≥ (n^2 - 3n + 2)/2.

Multiply both sides by 2:

2n^2 - 4n + 2k (A - n + 1) ≥ n^2 - 3n + 2.

Bring terms:

2n^2 - 4n - n^2 + 3n - 2 ≥ -2k (A - n + 1) => (n^2 - n - 2) ≥ -2k (A - n + 1).

Actually compute:

2n^2 - 4n + 2k (A - n + 1) - (n^2 - 3n + 2) ≥ 0 => (2n^2 - n^2) + (-4n + 3n) + ( -2) + 2k (A - n + 1) ≥ 0 => n^2 - n - 2 + 2k (A - n + 1) ≥ 0.

So 2k (A - n + 1) ≥ -(n^2 - n - 2) = -n^2 + n + 2.

Thus k (A - n + 1) ≥ (-n^2 + n + 2)/2.

Now, A - n + 1 = ⌊(n+1)/2⌋ - n + 1. Let's compute:

Case n even: n = 2t. Then A = t. So A - n + 1 = t - 2t + 1 = 1 - t = -(t-1). That's negative. So left side is negative. Right side is (-n^2 + n + 2)/2, which is negative for large n. So this inequality is something like k * (negative) ≥ negative. That could be satisfied for many k. Let's solve:

k * (A - n + 1) ≥ \frac{-n^2 + n + 2}{2}.

Since A - n + 1 is negative, dividing by it flips inequality. So we get:

k ≤ \frac{-n^2 + n + 2}{2 (A - n + 1)}.

But A - n + 1 is negative, so the denominator is negative, making the fraction something like? Let's compute numeric for n=4: t=2, A=2, A-n+1 = 2-4+1 = -1. -n^2+n+2 = -16+4+2 = -10. Then k ≤ (-10) / (2 * (-1)) = (-10) / (-2) = 5. That's trivial. So this doesn't bound.

But we want a lower bound on m, so perhaps we need a different invariant.

Maybe we can consider the points with a+b = 2 (i.e., (1,1)). That's one point. It can be covered by any line passing through it. Not helpful.

- For i = 1,2,..., t where t = something like ⌈n/2⌉? Actually we need to cover all points. Perhaps we can take the following non-sunny lines:

- For each non-sunny line L, |L ∩ I_n| ≤ C, where C = n-2 (max). But is it possible that a non-sunny line covers many interior points? Yes, as we said, a line like x=2 (vertical) covers interior points with a=2, b from 2 to n-1? Actually for n=5, a=2 covers b=2..4? But need a+b ≤6, so b ≤4. So points (2,2),(2,3),(2,4) are interior (a=2,b≥2). That's 3 points. n-2 =3. So indeed max is n-2. Similarly, a line like y=2 covers interior points with b=2, a from 2 to n-1? For n=5, a from 2 to 4? (2,2),(3,2),(4,2) → 3 points. So yes. A line like x+y=6 (slope -1) covers interior points with a,b≥2, a from 2 to 4? For n=5, a=2→b=4, a=3→b=3, a=4→b=2, that's 3 points, n-2=3. So we need to consider that the m non-sunny lines we choose can be placed to cover as many interior points as possible, but we also have to cover all points, so we need to cover the entire set I_n. The total capacity from non-sunny lines (max distinct interior points they can cover) is at most m * (n-2). But they might also cover points on the axes. That doesn't matter. The sunny lines have max interior points per line of B = ⌊(n+1)/2⌋ - 1.

Thus, we have:

|I_n| ≤ m * (n-2) + k * B.

Now, |I_n| = (n-1)(n-2)/2.

So inequality:

(n-1)(n-2)/2 ≤ m (n-2) + k B.

Since m = n - k, substitute:

(n-1)(n-2)/2 ≤ (n - k)(n-2) + k B.

Simplify:

(n-1)(n-2)/2 ≤ (n-2)n - k (n-2) + k B = n(n-2) + k (B - n + 2).

Thus:

k (B - n + 2) ≥ (n-1)(n-2)/2 - n(n-2).

Compute RHS:

n(n-2) = n^2 - 2n.
(n-1)(n-2)/2 = (n^2 - 3n + 2)/2.
So difference: (n-1)(n-2)/2 - n(n-2) = \frac{n^2 - 3n + 2 - 2n^2 + 4n}{2} = \frac{-n^2 + n + 2}{2} = \frac{-(n^2 - n - 2)}{2}.

So RHS = \frac{-(n^2 - n - 2)}{2}.

Now, B - n + 2 = (⌊(n+1)/2⌋ - 1) - n + 2 = ⌊(n+1)/2⌋ - n + 1.

Compute:

If n even: n = 2t, then ⌊(n+1)/2⌋ = t, so B - n + 2 = t - 2t + 1 = 1 - t = -(t-1). That's negative.
If n odd: n = 2t+1, then ⌊(n+1)/2⌋ = t+1, so B - n + 2 = (t+1) - (2t+1) + 1 = t+1 - 2t -1 +1 = -t +1 = 1 - t. That's also negative. So indeed, B - n + 2 is negative for all n≥3? Let's compute numeric: n=3 → t=1 → 1 - 1 = 0, B = floor(4/2)-1 = 2-1=1, B - n + 2 = 1 - 3 + 2 = 0. n=4 → t=2 → 1-2 = -1. n=5 → t=2 → 1-2 = -1. So for n=3, it's 0. That's interesting. So for n=3, the coefficient is zero, so the inequality becomes 0 ≥ RHS, which is? RHS = (-(9-3-2)/2) = (-4)/2 = -2. So 0 ≥ -2 holds. So that doesn't give a contradiction. So for n=3, m could be as low as 2, and k = 1, which satisfies the bound. So the inequality is not restrictive enough to rule out k > ⌊(n-1)/2⌋ for n=3? Actually ⌊(n-1)/2⌋=1, and k=1 is allowed. So it's okay. For n=4, m≥3, which we need to prove. The inequality with interior points might give a lower bound on m. Let's compute for n=4:

n=4 → t=2, n-2=2, B = ⌊5/2⌋ - 1 = 2-1=1. So inequality: (3*2)/2 = 3 ≤ m*2 + k*1. m = n - k = 4 - k. So 3 ≤ (4 - k)*2 + k = 8 - 2k + k = 8 - k. Thus k ≤ 5. That doesn't restrict. But we need to show that m ≥ 3, i.e., k ≤ 1. So this inequality is too weak. However, we can try to improve the bound on interior points per sunny line. Thus, the inequality we derived is an upper bound on how many interior points can be covered by the lines, but we need a lower bound on the coverage of interior points from lines. Perhaps we can argue by considering the points (1,1), (1,2), ..., (1,n) and (2,1), (3,1), ..., (n,1). That's 2n-1 points. These are the "edge" points. For each such point, it must lie on some line. The idea is to bound the total number of edge points that can be covered by the m non-sunny lines. Since each non-sunny line is either horizontal, vertical, or slope -1. A horizontal line y = c can cover points with that y-coordinate, including (a,c) where a≥1. That's an edge point if c is small? Actually edge points are those with a=1 or b=1. A horizontal line y = c will cover points (a,c) for a≥1. For c=1, it covers all (a,1) which are edge points (b=1). For c=2, it covers (a,2) where a≥1. Among those, only (1,2) is an edge point (a=1). So a horizontal line can cover at most one edge point from the a=1 column, and many from the b=1 row if c=1. Similarly, a vertical line x = d covers points (d,b) for b≥1. For d=1, it covers all (1,b) which are edge points (a=1). For d=2, it covers (2,b) where (2,1) is edge point (b=1). So each non-sunny line can cover many edge points. But we need to bound the total number of edge points that can be covered by m non-sunny lines. Since edge points are many, maybe we can prove that if m is too small, then the number of edge points that can be covered by non-sunny lines is insufficient, and the sunny lines cannot cover the remaining edge points because each sunny line can cover at most something like ⌊n/2⌋ edge points. Let's compute.

Edge points E_n = {(a,b): a≥1,b≥1, a+b ≤ n+1, (a=1 or b=1)}. Size = 2n-1. They are all points with a=1 or b=1. Some points are both (1,1). So 2n-1 distinct points.

Now, each line L (non-sunny or sunny) can contain at most some number of edge points. Let's bound for a sunny line L. If L is sunny, it's not parallel to x-axis, y-axis, or x+y=0. So it can have slope s ≠ 0, ∞, -1. Consider the edge points. The edge points are all points with a=1 (column) or b=1 (row). A line that is not horizontal or vertical can intersect the column a=1 at at most one point (since a=1 determines a vertical line, but a line with slope not infinite will intersect the line a=1 at exactly one point). Similarly, it can intersect the row b=1 at at most one point. So a sunny line can contain at most 2 edge points (one on a=1, one on b=1). However, is it possible that a sunny line contains a point that is on both a=1 and b=1 (i.e., (1,1))? That would be one point. So max edge points per sunny line is 2. That's a strong bound.

Now, for non-sunny lines, they can contain many edge points. For example, the line x=1 (vertical) contains all points (1,b) with b≤n, which are n edge points (a=1). Similarly, y=1 (horizontal) contains n edge points (b=1). A line of slope -1, say x+y = n+1, contains points with a+b = n+1. Among those, the edge points are those where a=1 or b=1. That's (1,n) and (n,1). So that's 2 edge points. So the most efficient non-sunny lines for covering edge points are the vertical and horizontal lines. So if we have m non-sunny lines, they can cover at most something like 2m? But they can be chosen to be vertical/horizontal. However, note that we have n lines total, and we can decide which are non-sunny. To minimize the number of non-sunny lines, we might want to use only non-sunny lines that cover many edge points. So if m is small, the total number of edge points that can be covered by non-sunny lines is at most (something) times m. But we also have sunny lines that can cover up to 2 edge points each. So we can bound the total edge points coverage.

Now, consider the edge points E_n. They must be covered by the n lines. The sum over lines of |L ∩ E_n| ≥ |E_n| = 2n-1.

We have m non-sunny lines and k sunny lines. Let's denote:

- For each non-sunny line L, |L ∩ E_n| ≤ n (if it's x=1 or y=1), but also could be less if it's something else. But we can bound it by n. However, to get a contradiction when m is small, we can assume that the non-sunny lines are chosen to maximize edge coverage, but they must also cover interior points. But we can use an upper bound on the total edge coverage that is possible with m non-sunny lines, given that they are distinct lines. But we need a lower bound on the coverage required. Since the required coverage is 2n-1, and the maximum coverage from sunny lines is k * 2. So if m is small, the total coverage from non-sunny lines must be at least (2n-1) - 2k. But we also have an upper bound on coverage from non-sunny lines: each non-sunny line can cover at most n edge points. That's not restrictive. However, we can also consider that non-sunny lines are limited in number, and they cannot all be vertical/horizontal because they must be distinct lines. For example, we could have vertical lines x=1, x=2, ..., x=m. Those each cover some edge points. The edge points on column a=1 are n points (1,b). The vertical line x=1 covers all n. The vertical line x=2 covers only (2,1) (edge point) because (2,1) is b=1; (2,b) for b≥2 are not edge points (since a=2, b≥2 => not a=1 nor b=1). So the number of edge points on x=2 is 1 (if b=1) plus maybe (2,1) is covered, but also (2,? Actually edge points with b=1 are (a,1) for a≥1. So vertical line x=2 covers (2,1) which is an edge point. So for a vertical line x=d, the edge points it covers are: (d,1) if d≥1 (b=1) and also (d,? Actually if d=1, it covers all (1,b) with b≤n, which are edge points because a=1. For d>1, it covers (d,1) only, and (d,? maybe (d,1) is edge point. Also if d=1, it covers (1,b) for b≤n, but also (1,1) counted. So coverage from vertical lines: x=1 gives n edge points; x=2 gives 1; x=3 gives 1; ... up to x=n gives 1. So total edge points from m vertical lines (assuming we pick the first m) would be n + (m-1). Similarly, horizontal lines: y=1 gives n edge points; y=2 gives 1; etc. So we can cover many edge points with few non-sunny lines by using vertical/horizontal lines.

But we have to cover all points. The problem is to determine all possible k. Perhaps we can prove that k ≤ ⌊(n-1)/2⌋ by a simple pairing argument: For each sunny line, we can consider some kind of "inversion" with respect to the triangle. Another approach: Consider the set of points (a,b) with a+b = n+1 (hypotenuse). There are n points. Each sunny line can cover at most 1 of them. So if we have k sunny lines, they can cover at most k hypotenuse points. The remaining n - k hypotenuse points must be covered by non-sunny lines. That's fine. But maybe we can consider the points (a,b) with a+b = n (the next layer). There are n-1 points. Sunny lines can cover at most 1 each, so they can cover at most k points in that layer. The non-sunny lines can also cover points in that layer. But the total points in that layer is n-1. So if k is large, then the number of points in that layer covered by sunny lines is at most k, but they might cover many. However, we need to cover all points. So if k is large, then the number of non-sunny lines is small, and they must cover many points. But we can bound the maximum number of points that can be covered by m non-sunny lines in the triangle. Perhaps we can use a double counting of incidences of lines with points, but with a more precise bound on the maximum number of points a non-sunny line can cover (which is n), and a more precise bound on the maximum number of points a sunny line can cover (which is ⌊(n+1)/2⌋). However, note that the upper bound on coverage per line is not the maximum possible points on that line within P_n, but it's an upper bound. That's what we used. The inequality N_n ≤ m*n + k*A holds. This inequality is a necessary condition. It is not sufficient, but if it fails, impossible. So to prove impossibility for large k, we need to show that N_n > m*n + k*A. Let's test for n=4, k=2 (which we suspect impossible). m=2, A=⌊5/2⌋=2. Then m*n + k*A = 2*4 + 2*2 = 8+4=12. N_n=10. So inequality holds (10 ≤ 12). So that doesn't rule out. For n=4, k=3 (m=1) gives 1*4 + 3*2 = 4+6=10, N_n=10, holds. So that inequality doesn't forbid k=3. But we know that for n=4, k=3 is impossible. So we need a stronger condition. So we need a different necessary condition, maybe involving the fact that some points cannot be covered by any sunny line if the number of sunny lines is too high because of some combinatorial limitation? That seems unlikely.

Let's attempt to prove the upper bound directly by considering the points with a+b = n+1 and a+b = n. Suppose we have k sunny lines. For each sunny line, it can contain at most 1 point from the set H (hypotenuse). So the sunny lines cover at most k points of H. Actually each non-sunny line can cover at most n points of H (if it's a slope -1 line), but if we use many non-sunny lines, they might be of types that cover at most 1 each. Now, perhaps we need to consider points with a+b = n (second layer). There are n-1 points. Sunny lines can cover at most 1 each, so at most k. So maybe the difficulty is not about edge points, but about the interior points that are not on the axes. The interior points form a triangle of size (n-2). To cover those, we need many non-sunny lines or many sunny lines. But we already argued that each sunny line can cover at most ⌊(n+1)/2⌋ - 1 interior points. That's about n/2 - 1. So with k sunny lines, they can cover at most k * (A-1) interior points. With m non-sunny lines, they can cover at most m * (n-2) interior points. The total interior points are (n-1)(n-2)/2. So we need:

k (A-1) + m (n-2) ≥ (n-1)(n-2)/2.

Now, m = n - k. Plug in:

k (A-1) + (n - k)(n-2) ≥ (n-1)(n-2)/2.

Simplify. Compute (n - k)(n-2) = n(n-2) - k(n-2). So inequality becomes:

k (A-1) + n(n-2) - k(n-2)) ≥ (n-1)(n-2)/2 → n(n-2) + k(A-1 - (n-2)) ≥ (n-1)(n-2)/2.

Now, A = ⌊(n+1)/2⌋. Compute A - 1 - (n-2) = A - n + 1. As before. So we get:

n(n-2) + k (A - n + 1) ≥ (n-1)(n-2)/2.

Now, compute RHS. For n=4, n(n-2)=4*2=8. (n-1)(n-2)/2=3*2/2=3. So inequality is 8 + k*(A - n + 1) ≥ 3. A - n + 1 = 2 - 4 + 1 = -1. So 8 - k ≥ 3 → k ≤ 5. So not restrictive. For n=5, n(n-2)=5*3=15. (n-1)(n-2)/2=4*3/2=6. A - n + 1 = 3 - 5 + 1 = -1. So 15 - k ≥ 6 → k ≤ 9. So again not restrictive.

Thus, interior points inequality doesn't forbid large k.

So we need a more sophisticated necessary condition. Let's try to understand the geometry of covering a triangular grid with lines. We already computed that inequality. But it seems too weak to give a bound. The inequality is an upper bound on the possible coverage of interior points from sunny lines? Actually it's a lower bound on the coverage needed: The sum of interior points covered by the lines must be at least |I_n|. The coverage from a sunny line is at most B, but it could be less. So we need to ensure that the maximum possible total coverage from sunny lines (which is k B) plus the maximum possible from non-sunny lines (m (n-2)) is at least |I_n|. That's a necessary condition. If it fails, impossible. But we saw it's satisfied for many k. However, we need to prove impossibility for large k. So perhaps the inequality is not sufficient, but we can derive a stronger bound by considering that not only the maximum coverage is needed, but also that the lines must be distinct and that the coverage cannot be achieved simultaneously in a way that covers all interior points because of some structural constraints. So with 4 sunny lines, maximum coverage is 4*2=8, which is less than 10. So we cannot cover all 10 points with 4 sunny lines. Therefore, m=1 is impossible. So for n=5, k=4 is impossible.

Now, what about k=3? That's m=2. We found a construction with m=2 (x=1,y=1) and k=3 sunny lines. That's feasible. So maximum k is at least 3, and we suspect that k=4 is impossible. Let's test with n=5, k=4 (m=1) impossible. So the maximum k is 3? But earlier we argued that k ≤ ⌊(n-1)/2⌋ = 2. That was false. So we need to determine the actual bound. Let's compute for n=5, ⌊(n-1)/2⌋ = 2. But we have k=3 possible. So the bound is higher. Let's try to find a pattern.

From n=3: max k = 1 (k=2 impossible). That's ⌊(n-1)/2⌋? For n=3, (3-1)/2 = 1 → matches. For n=4: max k = 1 (k=2 impossible). That's (4-1)/2 = 1.5 → floor = 1, matches. For n=5: max k = 3? Let's see if k=3 is possible. We constructed. Could k=4 be possible? That seems impossible. So the maximum k is 3. That's larger than floor((n-1)/2) = 2. So maybe the bound is something like ⌊(2n-1)/3⌋? Not sure.

Let's examine the construction for n=5 with k=3. That used 3 sunny lines covering interior points. The interior points were covered by three sunny lines that are pairwise intersecting and each passes through two interior points. That seems to be a covering of the interior triangle (size 3) with 3 lines. That's possible because the interior triangle (with a,b≥2) is a triangle of side length 3 (in terms of coordinates). The maximum number of points a sunny line can cover in that triangle is ⌊(n+1)/2⌋ - 1 = 2. So with 3 sunny lines, we can cover up to 6 points, which is exactly the number of interior points. So we need to cover the interior triangle with 3 sunny lines. That seems feasible if we can partition the 6 points into 3 pairs each collinear with a sunny line. In the case n=5, we found three such pairs: (A,E), (B,F), (C,D). That's three lines. So that works. For n=4, the interior triangle (a,b≥2) is 3 points. To cover them with sunny lines, we would need at least 2 sunny lines (since each sunny line can cover at most 1 interior point). That's possible. But in the example we had for n=4 with k=1, we used 3 non-sunny lines and 1 sunny. That's m=3,k=1. Could we have m=2,k=2 for n=4? We argued impossible because with 2 non-sunny lines, the uncovered set has size 3 and those 3 points cannot be covered by 2 sunny lines. So k=2 impossible. So for n=4, maximum k = 1, which is floor((n-1)/2) = 1. For n=5, floor((n-1)/2) = 2, but we have k=3. So the bound is not that.

Let's compute the maximum possible k for n=5 using a similar reasoning. The interior points are 6. The maximum points per sunny line is 2. So if we have k sunny lines, they can cover at most 2k interior points. The non-sunny lines can cover at most (n - k) * (n-2) interior points. The total interior points must be covered. So we need:

2k + (n - k)*(n-2) ≥ |I_n| = (n-1)(n-2)/2.

For n=5, that's 2k + (5 - k)*3 ≥ 6 → 2k + 15 - 3k ≥ 6 → 15 - k ≥ 6 → k ≤ 9. That's not restrictive. So that doesn't bound k.

The real constraint seems to be about covering the "edges" of the triangle (the points on the axes) with sunny lines, given that sunny lines can cover at most 2 such points each. But we also have non-sunny lines. The edge points are 2n-1. The sunny lines can cover at most 2k edge points. The non-sunny lines can cover at most something like 2m (if they are x=1 and y=1), but also they can cover many edge points if placed appropriately. However, if m is small, the non-sunny lines are few, and they may not be able to cover all edge points. Let's analyze the edge points (E_n) with size 2n-1. The non-sunny lines we have are m lines. They could be chosen to be x=1 and y=1 (two lines) covering all edge points. That's m=2. So with m=2, we can cover all edge points. With m=1, we can cover at most n edge points (if it's x=1 or y=1). That leaves n-1 edge points uncovered. For n=5, that's 4 uncovered. With m=1, we have k=4 sunny lines, which can cover at most 2k = 8 edge points, but they also need to cover interior points. The uncovered edge points after the non-sunny line are those with a=1 or b=1 except those covered by that line. If we choose non-sunny line to be x=1, then covered edge points are (1,b) for b≤n, that's n points. The remaining edge points are (a,1) with a≥2, that's n-1 points. Those are on the b=1 axis. So we need to cover those with sunny lines. Sunny lines can cover at most 1 each (since they cannot be horizontal). So with k=4 sunny lines, they could cover up to 4 such points. That's enough to cover all n-1 = 4 points. So edge points are coverable. The interior points are 6. Sunny lines can cover at most 2 each, so 4*2 = 8, enough. So why did we earlier think that k=4 is impossible? Because we tried to cover P_4 (10 points) with 4 sunny lines, which would be insufficient if we used only sunny lines. But that scenario was when we used a non-sunny line covering hypotenuse (x+y=6) leaving P_4 uncovered. That's a different choice. In the configuration with m=1, we are free to choose which line is non-sunny. We could choose it to be x=1 (or y=1). That would leave a set of 10 points (those with a≥2) that includes interior points and also some edge points (the b=1 row). That's 10 points. With 4 sunny lines, we can cover up to 12 points, so capacity is enough. But we need to check if there exists a set of 4 sunny lines covering those 10 points. The 10 points are:

(2,1),(2,2),(2,3),(2,4)
(3,1),(3,2),(3,3)
(4,1),(4,2)
(5,1)

Now, we need 4 sunny lines. We already have three sunny lines from the previous construction that covered interior points (A,E), (B,F), (C,D). Those are:

L1: y = x (covers (2,2),(3,3))
L2: y = -x/2 + 4 (covers (2,3),(4,2))
L3: y = -2x + 8 (covers (2,4),(3,2))

Those are sunny. Now, what remains uncovered from the set of 10 points? The uncovered points are: (2,1),(3,1),(4,1),(5,1). That's 4 points. We have one more sunny line (L4). Can we cover these 4 points with one sunny line? A sunny line can contain at most 1 of them (since any line passing through two of them is horizontal, not sunny). So we cannot cover all 4 with one sunny line. However, we could choose different sunny lines that also cover some of those edge points. We are not forced to use L1,L2,L3; we can design a covering with 4 sunny lines that covers all 10 points. The question is: is there any covering of P_5 with 4 sunny lines and 1 non-sunny line (like x=1) that covers all points? That would be a set of 5 lines, with 4 sunny, 1 non-sunny. We need to see if such a covering exists. The previous attempt with three sunny lines covering interior points plus one sunny line covering some edge points but not all failed. But maybe we can use a different combination of sunny lines that covers both interior points and edge points more efficiently. For instance, we could use lines like y = x, y = x-1, y = x-2, y = x-3. Those are sunny. Do they cover all points? Let's try to cover P_5 with lines of slope 1 (sunny). The points with a=b are on y=x. The points with a=b+1 are on y = x-1. The points with a=b+2 are on y = x-2. The points with a=b+3 are on y = x-3. The points with a=b+4 are on y = x-4, but those are not in P_5. So we can cover all points with slopes 1 lines? Let's list points:

(1,1): y=x
(1,2): y = x+1 (not slope 1)
(1,3): y = x+2
(1,4): y = x+3
(1,5): y = x+4
(2,1): y = x-1
(2,2): y=x
(2,3): y=x+1
(2,4): y=x+2

(3,2): y=x-1
(3,3): y=x
(4,1): y=x-3
(4,2): y=x-2
(5,1): y=x-4

So we see that points like (1,2),(1,3),(1,4),(1,5) have a=1, b>1. They are not on any line of slope 1 (except (1,1)). So we would need lines with other slopes to cover them. So covering with all slope 1 lines fails. So we need a mixture.

Thus, the question is: for n=5, what is the maximum number of sunny lines possible? We have a construction with k=3. Could we have k=4? Let's try to design a covering with 4 sunny lines and 1 non-sunny line. Suppose we take non-sunny line to be x+y=6 (hypotenuse). That covers 5 points. The remaining 10 points are P_4. Now suppose we take non-sunny line to be x=1. That covers 5 points. The remaining 10 points are as listed. We need to cover them with 4 sunny lines. Let's try to see if it's possible. Therefore, each sunny line can cover at most 1 edge point. So to cover all 4 edge points (after using x=1), we need at least 4 sunny lines. That would be k=4. With 4 sunny lines, we have enough capacity. The question is: can we arrange 4 sunny lines such that they collectively contain all 6 interior points and all 4 edge points (the b=1 row)? That's a covering of the 10-point set. Since each sunny line can contain at most 2 interior points (B=2), and at most 1 edge point, the total capacity for interior points is 8, for edge points is 4. - Each line contains at most 1 edge point from the set (since if it contained 2, it would be horizontal). So each line can contain at most 1 edge point.

- The lines collectively contain all 6 interior points.

Thus, we need to partition the 4 edge points among the 4 lines (each line gets one), and also each line must contain some interior points (perhaps multiple). That seems plausible.

But we also need to ensure that the lines are distinct and sunny. Let's try to construct.

L1: y = x - 1 (covers (2,1))

- L3: y = x - 3 passes through (4,1), (5,2) – (5,2) sum=7 >6, not included. So covers (4,1) and (5,2) but (5,2) is not in P_5? Wait (5,2) sum=7 >6, so not in P_5. So L3 covers (4,1) only? Actually (5,2) is not in P_5. So L3 covers (4,1) only.
- L4: y = x - 4 passes through (5,1) – (6,2) sum=8 >6, not included. So covers (5,1) only.

Thus, these four lines cover the four edge points (2,1),(3,1),(4,1),(5,1) and also some interior points: (3,2),(4,2) are covered. But we still need to cover the interior points (2,2),(2,3),(2,4),(3,3),(4,2)? Actually (4,2) is covered by L2. (3,2) covered by L1. So covered interior points: (3,2),(4,2). But we have already used 4 sunny lines. We cannot add more lines. So this covering fails. However, we could try a different assignment of lines. Perhaps we can use lines that are not all of slope 1, but a mix that covers both edge and interior points efficiently. Actually the maximum points a non-sunny line can cover is n (if it's axis-aligned with intercept 1, or slope -1 with sum = n+1). That's 5. So after that, uncovered is 10. Could we modify the lines to cover more interior points? For example, we could use lines with different slopes that still pass through the edge points. - For E1: y=x-1 covers A? (2,2) gives 2=2-1? 2 = 1? No. (2,2) is on y=x, not x-1. So y=x-1 covers (2,1) and (3,2). That's E1 and D (3,2). So covers D.

Actually we can try slope 1/2: y = (1/2)x - 1/2, which is not integer, so it won't hit any other lattice points in the set. Since E3 is (4,1), interior points are: A(2,2), B(2,3), C(2,4), D(3,2), E(3,3), F(4,2). Let's check if any of these are collinear with (4,1) on a line with slope not 0,∞,-1. Compute slopes:

- A (2,2): slope = (2-1)/(2-4) = 1/(-2) = -1/2. That's sunny. So line through (4,1) and (2,2) has slope -1/2. That's allowed. Does it contain any other points? Possibly (6,0) not in set. So that's one interior point.

Another idea: Use a double counting of pairs (point, line) where the point lies on the line, but also consider the fact that each non-sunny line can cover many points, but each sunny line can cover at most something. The total incidences must be at least N_n. That gives:

∑_{L} |L ∩ P_n| ≥ N_n.

N_n ≤ m n + k ⌊(n+1)/2⌋.

Since m = n - k, we can solve for k. This inequality is necessary. But as we saw, it gives a weak bound. But maybe it's sufficient to prove that k cannot exceed something like n - 1? But is it possible that some sunny line covers 2 points from S, but also some sunny lines cover 1 point, and overall with 4 lines we could cover 10 points if each covers 2.5 on average. So this argument relies on the fact that the complement S has size at least 10, and that each sunny line can cover at most 2 points from S. Let's try to derive a necessary condition for covering. Suppose we have a covering with m non-sunny lines and k sunny lines. Consider the set of points with a,b ≥ 2. This set has size I_n = (n-1)(n-2)/2. These points must be covered by the lines. The non-sunny lines can cover some of them. The sunny lines can cover some. The maximum number of points from I_n that a sunny line can cover is something like B = ⌊(n+1)/2⌋ - 1. Let's denote L = ⌊(n+1)/2⌋ - 1. For n even, n=2t, L = t - 1. For n odd, n=2t+1, L = t.

Now, each non-sunny line can cover at most n-2 points from I_n (since axis-aligned lines with intercept 2 cover n-2 points). But is that always the maximum? A non-sunny line of type H (horizontal) y = c covers points with b = c. For it to cover points in I_n, we need c ≥ 2. Then the number of points in I_n on that line is at most (n+1 - c) - 2 + 1 = n - c. The maximum occurs at c = 2, giving n-2. Similarly, vertical lines x = d give n-2 at d=2. Slope -1 lines D_e: points with a+b = e, e ≥ 4. The number of points in I_n on D_e is at most (e-3) (since a from 2 to e-2). The maximum occurs at e = n+1, giving (n+1)-3 = n-2. So indeed, any non-sunny line can cover at most n-2 points from I_n. So we have:

|I_n| ≤ m * (n-2) + k * L.

Now, |I_n| = (n-1)(n-2)/2. So:

(n-1)(n-2)/2 ≤ m (n-2) + k L.

Plug m = n - k:

(n-1)(n-2)/2 ≤ (n - k)(n-2) + k L.

Compute RHS = (n-2)n - k(n-2) + k L = n(n-2) + k (L - n + 2).

Thus:

k (L - n + 2) ≥ (n-1)(n-2)/2 - n(n-2).

Compute RHS:

n(n-2) = n^2 - 2n.
(n-1)(n-2)/2 = (n^2 - 3n + 2)/2.
Difference = (n^2 - 3n + 2)/2 - (n^2 - 2n) = (n^2 - 3n + 2 - 2n^2 + 4n)/2 = (-n^2 + n + 2)/2.

So:

k (L - n + 2) ≥ (-n^2 + n + 2)/2.

Now, L - n + 2 = (⌊(n+1)/2⌋ - 1) - n + 2 = ⌊(n+1)/2⌋ - n + 1.

Compute:

If n is even, n = 2t: ⌊(2t+1)/2⌋ = t, so L - n + 2 = t - 2t + 1 = -t + 1 = 1 - t. This is negative for t ≥ 2. For t=2 (n=4), it's 1-2 = -1. For t=3 (n=6), it's 1-3 = -2. For t=1 (n=2) not considered.

If n is odd, n = 2t+1: ⌊(2t+2)/2⌋ = t+1, so L = t, then L - n + 2 = t - (2t+1) + 2 = t - 2t - 1 + 2 = -t + 1 = 1 - t. For t=1 (n=3), it's 0. For t=2 (n=5), it's -1. So L - n + 2 is non-positive, and zero only for n=3. So the coefficient is negative for n ≥ 4. That means the inequality becomes:

k * (negative) ≥ (negative). Dividing by the negative flips inequality, giving:

k ≤ \frac{(-n^2 + n + 2)/2}{L - n + 2}.

But L - n + 2 is negative, so the fraction is something. Let's compute it for n=5: L = 2, L - n + 2 = 2 - 5 + 2 = -1. RHS numerator = (-25 + 5 + 2)/2 = (-18)/2 = -9. Then k ≤ (-9)/(-1) = 9. So trivial. So that inequality does not bound k.

Thus, the interior points inequality is too weak. Plug n=6: I_n = 5*4/2 = 10. Then 2k + (6 - k)*4 ≥ 10 → 2k + 24 - 4k ≥ 10 → 24 - 2k ≥ 10 → 2k ≤ 14 → k ≤ 7. So not restrictive.

But we also have the axis points (E_n) of size 2n-1 = 11. The sunny lines can cover at most 2k axis points. The non-sunny lines can cover at most m * n (but axis points are efficiently covered by axis-aligned lines). For n=6, we need to cover 11 axis points. With m non-sunny lines, we can cover many. But the difficulty might be that the complement after using m non-sunny lines (points not on any non-sunny line) must be covered by sunny lines. The complement's size is N_n - |union|. The maximum union from m non-sunny lines is something like? If m is small, the complement is large. For m=1, max union = n = 6, complement ≥ 21 - 6 = 15. For m=2, max union = 2n - 1 = 11, complement ≥ 10. For m=3, max union ≤ 3n - 3 = 15, complement ≥ 6. For m=4, max union ≤ 4n - 6? Actually we need to compute maximum distinct points from m lines of types H,V,D. The maximum union is achieved by taking lines that are as "independent" as possible, with minimal overlaps. Since each line can cover at most n points, and intersections are at most 1, the maximum union from m lines is at most m*n - (m choose 2) (if all pairs intersect in distinct points, and each intersection is counted twice). But this bound is only valid if the lines are such that each line has n points and they intersect pairwise in distinct points. However, for m large, the lines may not be able to achieve that because some lines may be parallel and have no intersection. But we can try to maximize union by taking lines that are all of the type that cover n points, and that are in general position (no two parallel). The maximum union size from m lines (with each line covering n points) is at most m*n - C(m,2), because each pair of lines shares at most 1 point, and those shared points are distinct if the lines are in general position. However, is it possible that all pairs intersect in distinct points? Yes, if the lines are not parallel and no three are concurrent. That's a typical configuration of lines in general position. For example, take lines x = 1, x = 2, ..., x = m. Those are vertical, each covers n points. But they are parallel, so they share no points. That gives union size m*n. But they are all vertical, which is allowed (they are non-sunny). However, they are all of the same type (vertical) and are parallel, so they intersect in no points. That's good. But can we have a set of m vertical lines that are all distinct, each covering n points? For n=6, the vertical line x = d covers points (d,b) with b up to 7-d (since a+b ≤7). So if d is large, the number of points is less than n. For d=1, it covers 6 points (since b ≤6). For d=2, it covers 5 points (b ≤5). So to have n points, we need d ≤ n. Actually the line x = d covers points with b ≤ n+1 - d. To cover n points, we need n+1 - d ≥ n → d ≤ 1. So only x=1 gives n points. Similarly, horizontal lines y=1 give n points. So we cannot have many lines covering n points. So the maximum union from m non-sunny lines is achieved by taking lines that cover as many points as possible, but they may not all achieve n points. So we need to compute the maximum possible union size for given m, over choices of lines (each of types H,V,D). This is a combinatorial geometry problem. The problem likely expects a solution that uses a pairing argument on the "boundary" points or something like that, leading to a bound on k in terms of n. The construction for n=5 with k=3 and n=3 with k=1 and n=4 with k=1 suggests that the maximum k is something like: if n is odd, max k = n-2? That would be 3 for n=5, which matches. For n=3, n-2 = 1, matches. For n=5, we have k=3. For n=7, would k=5 be possible? We need to check. The bound from the interior points might be that with m = ⌊n/2⌋? Let's try to derive a lower bound on m. Suppose we have a covering with n lines. Consider the points with a+b = n+1 (hypotenuse). There are n such points. Each sunny line can contain at most 1 of them. So if we have k sunny lines, they can cover at most k points on the hypotenuse. The remaining n - k points on the hypotenuse must be covered by non-sunny lines. Now, a non-sunny line can cover at most 2 points on the hypotenuse? Actually a line of slope -1 can cover all n points if it's x+y = n+1. That's one line. A horizontal line y = c covers at most 1 point on the hypotenuse (the one with that y). A vertical line covers at most 1. So to cover the n - k hypotenuse points, we could use one line (hypotenuse) covering all n. That's one non-sunny line. So m can be as low as 1, covering the hypotenuse. So that doesn't bound.

Now consider the points with a+b = n (the second layer). There are n-1 points. A sunny line can cover at most 1 of them. So with k sunny lines, they can cover at most k points in that layer. The remaining n-1 - k must be covered by non-sunny lines. A non-sunny line can cover at most 1 point in that layer (if it's horizontal or vertical) or many if it's slope -1? Actually a line of slope -1 with sum = n would cover points with a+b = n. That's exactly that layer. So one such line could cover all n-1 points. That's one non-sunny line. So again, m can be 1.

But we also have the points on the axes. The axis points are many. The sunny lines can cover at most 2 of them each. So with k sunny lines, the maximum axis points covered is 2k. So perhaps the bound is: if we have m non-sunny lines, the number of axis points covered by them is at most something. The maximum axis points covered by m non-sunny lines is something like: if we take m axis-aligned lines (x=1, x=2, ..., x=m) but those are parallel and cover only some axis points (each vertical line covers (d,1) as axis, and also (d,?) but the axis points with a=1 are only one column; the axis points with b=1 are many. So not many axis points. To cover axis points efficiently, we can use x=1 and y=1. That's two lines covering all axis points. So with m=2, we can cover all axis points. With m=3, we can cover more, but we can also cover some interior points.

The key seems to be the interior triangle I_n. The sunny lines have limited capacity to cover points from I_n. The non-sunny lines can cover I_n points efficiently. The minimum number of non-sunny lines needed to cover I_n (or the whole set) might be something like ⌈(n-1)/2⌉? Let's compute the covering of I_n with non-sunny lines. I_n is a triangle of side (n-1) after shifting? Actually I_n has points with a,b≥2, a+b≤n+1. This is a triangle. The maximum number of points a non-sunny line can cover in I_n is n-2. So to cover I_n with m non-sunny lines, we need m * (n-2) ≥ I_n = (n-1)(n-2)/2 → m ≥ (n-1)/2. So m ≥ ⌈(n-1)/2⌉. That's a lower bound on the number of non-sunny lines needed to cover all points of I_n, if we rely solely on non-sunny lines. But we also have sunny lines that can cover some I_n points. However, if we have many sunny lines, they can cover some I_n points, reducing the needed non-sunny lines. The bound we are trying to prove is that m ≥ ⌈(n-1)/2⌉, i.e., the number of non-sunny lines must be at least ⌈(n-1)/2⌉. That would give k ≤ n - ⌈(n-1)/2⌉ = ⌊(n+1)/2⌋? Let's compute:

If n is odd, n=2t+1, then ⌈(n-1)/2⌉ = ⌈2t/2⌉ = t. So m ≥ t. Then k ≤ n - t = t+1. That would be k ≤ t+1 = (n+1)/2. For n=5, that's ≤3. Indeed, we have k=3 possible. That's okay. If n is even, n=2t, then ⌈(n-1)/2⌉ = ⌈(2t-1)/2⌉ = t. So m ≥ t. Then k ≤ n - t = t = n/2. For n=4, that's k ≤ 2, but we found k=2 impossible. So the bound m ≥ ⌈(n-1)/2⌉ is not always true, because for n=4, we have a covering with m=3 (k=1), but m≥2? Actually ⌈(n-1)/2⌉ = ⌈3/2⌉ = 2. So the bound says m ≥ 2. That's true (m=3≥2). But it doesn't forbid k=2 (since k ≤ n - 2 = 2). However, we need a stronger bound: maybe m ≥ ⌈n/2⌉ + 1 for even n? But for n=4, ⌈n/2⌉ = 2, +1 = 3. That matches m=3. For n=6, would m≥4? That seems plausible. But we need to prove it.

Let's analyze the necessary condition for covering I_n. Suppose we have a covering with m non-sunny lines and k sunny lines. The I_n points (size = (n-1)(n-2)/2) must be covered. Each sunny line can cover at most L = ⌊(n+1)/2⌋ - 1 points from I_n. The non-sunny lines can cover at most (n-2) points each from I_n. So we have:

|I_n| ≤ m (n-2) + k L.   (1)

Now, m = n - k. Substitute:

(n-1)(n-2)/2 ≤ (n - k)(n-2) + k L = n(n-2) - k(n-2) + k L = n(n-2) + k (L - n + 2).

Thus:

k (L - n + 2) ≥ (n-1)(n-2)/2 - n(n-2) = (-n^2 + n + 2)/2.

Now, L - n + 2 = (⌊(n+1)/2⌋ - 1) - n + 2 = ⌊(n+1)/2⌋ - n + 1.

Compute:

If n is even, n = 2t: ⌊(2t+1)/2⌋ = t, so L - n + 2 = t - 2t + 1 = -t + 1 = 1 - t. For t≥2, this is negative. For n=4, t=2 → -1; for n=6, t=3 → -2; etc.

If n is odd, n = 2t+1: ⌊(2t+2)/2⌋ = t+1, so L - n + 2 = (t+1) - (2t+1) + 2 = t+1 - 2t -1 + 2 = -t + 2 = 2 - t. For t=1 (n=3), this is 1; for t=2 (n=5), it's 0; for t=3 (n=7), it's -1; etc.

Thus, the coefficient (L - n + 2) is non-positive for n≥4. For n=3, it's positive. So for n≥4, we have:

k * (negative) ≥ (negative). Dividing by the negative flips inequality, giving:

k ≤ \frac{(-n^2 + n + 2)/2}{L - n + 2}. Since denominator is negative, the fraction is something like (positive)/positive? Let's compute the magnitude.

For n even, L - n + 2 = 1 - n/2. The numerator is (-n^2 + n + 2)/2. For n=6, numerator = (-36+6+2)/2 = (-28)/2 = -14. Denominator = 1 - 3 = -2. Then k ≤ (-14)/(-2) = 7. So trivial.

So this inequality doesn't bound k. So the interior points bound is not sufficient.

Now, consider the axis points (E_n). There are 2n-1 axis points. Each sunny line can cover at most 2 axis points. Each non-sunny line can cover at most n axis points (if it's x=1 or y=1). But to get a bound on m, we can consider that the axis points must be covered. If m is small, then the number of axis points covered by non-sunny lines is limited, and the sunny lines can cover at most 2k axis points. So we have:

2n-1 ≤ 2k + m * (max axis points per non-sunny line). But max axis points per non-sunny line is n (if it's x=1 or y=1). However, we can also use non-sunny lines like x=2, which cover only 1 axis point (b=1). So to maximize coverage, we would use lines that cover many axis points. So the maximum number of axis points that can be covered by m non-sunny lines is at most m * n. But that's an upper bound, not a lower bound. Actually we need a lower bound on m because we are assuming a covering exists. If m is small, then the total axis coverage from non-sunny lines is at most m * n. Combined with sunny coverage, we need to cover 2n-1 points. So we have:

2n-1 ≤ 2k + m n.

Since m = n - k, we get:

2n-1 ≤ 2k + (n - k) n = 2k + n^2 - n k = n^2 + (2 - n) k.

That's a linear inequality in k. Solve for k:

2n-1 ≤ n^2 + (2 - n) k → (2 - n) k ≥ 2n-1 - n^2 = -(n^2 - 2n + 1) = -(n-1)^2.

Thus, k ≤ \frac{-(n-1)^2}{2 - n} = \frac{-(n-1)^2}{-(n-2)} = \frac{(n-1)^2}{n-2}. For n≥3, this is > n. So trivial.

So not helpful.

We need a more clever invariant. Let's examine the n=5 construction in detail. It used 2 non-sunny lines (x=1,y=1). That's m=2. The complement (points not on those lines) is I_n. It can cover up to n=6 axis points if it's x=1 or y=1, or up to 2 axis points if it's hypotenuse. So we could choose the non-sunny line to be x=1, covering

## Solution
We are given n ≥ 3. Let P_n = {(a,b) ∈ ℕ^2 : a,b ≥ 1, a+b ≤ n+1}. The points to be covered are exactly the lattice points in the right triangle with legs on the positive axes. There are |P_n| = n(n+1)/2 points.

We call a line sunny if it is not parallel to the x‑axis, the y‑axis, or the line x+y=0. Thus a line is sunny iff its slope is not 0, not undefined (vertical), and not –1.

We need to determine all non‑negative integers k for which there exist n distinct lines L_1,…,L_n in the plane such that  

* every point of P_n lies on at least one of these lines, and  
* exactly k of the lines are sunny.

---

### 1. Upper bound on k
We prove that k ≤ ⌊(n−1)/2⌋ for n≥3, except possibly for small n? Wait we need to check. But from the n=5 example we have k=3, which is greater than ⌊(n−1)/2⌋=2, so that would contradict. So the upper bound must be something else. Let's re-evaluate.

I earlier attempted to derive an upper bound, but then discovered that for n=5, k=3 is possible, so any bound of the form k ≤ ⌊(n−1)/2⌋ is false. So we need a more refined bound. Let's compute the possible k for n=5,6, etc. and look for a pattern.

Let’s denote the set of points that are not on any non‑sunny line (i.e., points that must be covered by sunny lines). The non‑sunny lines are of types H (horizontal), V (vertical), D (slope –1). Their union covers some points. The remaining points must be covered by sunny lines.

We can attempt to find a lower bound on the number of non‑sunny lines needed to cover all points, regardless of which lines are chosen. Then k ≤ n – that bound. So we need to determine the minimum possible m = n – k such that there exists a covering. We can try to prove that m ≥ ⌈(n+1)/2⌉? That would give k ≤ n – ⌈(n+1)/2⌉ = ⌊(n−1)/2⌋. But for n=5, that would be k ≤ 2, yet we have k=3 possible. So that's false. The bound must be larger for odd n, and smaller for even n? Let's compute:

n=3: ⌈(n+1)/2⌉ = 2, k ≤ 1 (true, k=1 possible)
n=4: ⌈(n+1)/2⌉ = 3, k ≤ 1 (true, k=1 possible)
n=5: ⌈(n+1)/2⌉ = 3, k ≤ 2 (false, k=3 possible)
So indeed, for odd n, the bound ⌈(n+1)/2⌉ is not valid. So we need a different argument.

Maybe the correct bound is k ≤ n – ⌈n/2⌉? That's ⌊n/2⌋. For n=5, that's 2, still false. So we need to find the actual maximum k. The example with n=5 and k=3 suggests that k can be as high as n−2 (since n=5, n−2=3). For n=3, n−2=1, matches. For n=4, n−2=2, but k=2 impossible. So it seems that when n is even, k cannot be n−2, but maybe can be n−3? For n=4, n−3=1, matches. For n=6, n−2=4, maybe k=4 impossible, but k=3 might be possible? Let's try to construct for n=6 with k=3. That would be m=3 non‑sunny lines. We need to cover 21 points. With 3 non‑sunny lines, the union size can be at most something. If we take the three non‑sunny lines to be x=1, y=1, and the hypotenuse x+y=7 (since n=6 → n+1=7). That's three lines. The union size we can compute. Let's compute quickly: x=1 covers 6 points (1,b) b≤6; y=1 covers 6 points (a,1) a≤6; hypotenuse covers 7 points (a+b=7). Overlaps: (1,6) and (6,1) are axis, already covered. The union may be 6+6+7 - (overlaps) = 19 - maybe 3? Actually let's compute properly for n=6. Points in P_6:

a,b≥1, a+b≤7. List:

(1,1)-(1,6) → 6
(2,1)-(2,5) → 5
(3,1)-(3,4) → 4
(4,1)-(4,3) → 3
(5,1)-(5,2) → 2
(6,1) → 1
(7,?) none.
Total N = 6+5+4+3+2+1 = 21.

Now, x=1 covers (1,1)-(1,6): 6 points.
y=1 covers (1,1),(2,1),(3,1),(4,1),(5,1),(6,1): 6 points.
Hypotenuse (x+y=7) covers (1,6),(2,5),(3,4),(4,3),(5,2),(6,1): 6 points. (since sum=7, and a,b≥1, maximum a is 6, b=1). So 6 points.

Now compute union:

From x=1: (1,1)-(1,6) → 6 points.
From y=1: (1,1),(2,1),(3,1),(4,1),(5,1),(6,1) → 6 points, but (1,1) already counted.
From hyp: (1,6),(2,5),(3,4),(4,3),(5,2),(6,1) → 6 points.

Now count distinct:

(1,1) appears in x=1 and y=1.
(1,2)-(1,5) appear only in x=1.
(1,6) appears in x=1 and hyp.
(2,1) appears only in y=1.
(3,1) only in y=1.
(4,1) only in y=1.
(5,1) only in y=1.
(6,1) appears in y=1 and hyp.
(2,5) appears only in hyp.
(3,4) only in hyp.
(4,3) only in hyp.
(5,2) only in hyp.

Also (2,2)-(2,4) are not covered? (2,2) sum=4 ≤7, but not on any of these lines. So they are uncovered. So union size is: 6 (x=1) + (from y=1, we have (2,1),(3,1),(4,1),(5,1) – that's 4 new, plus (1,1) already) + (from hyp, we have (2,5),(3,4),(4,3),(5,2) – that's 4 new, and (1,6),(6,1) already counted). So total = 6 + 4 + 4 = 14. So union size = 14. That leaves 7 points uncovered. Those are the points not on any of those lines. Let's list uncovered points: they are (2,2),(2,3),(2,4) (since (2,5) is on hyp, (2,1) covered), (3,2),(3,3) (3,2 not on hyp? (3,2) sum=5, not on hyp, not on axis), (4,2),(4,? ) Actually (4,2) sum=6, not on hyp; (4,3) on hyp; (4,4) sum=8>7 not in P. (5,2) on hyp; (5,3) sum=8>7 not in P; (6,1) covered. So uncovered are: (2,2),(2,3),(2,4),(3,2),(3,3),(4,2). That's 6 points. So complement size = 6. That's I_n for n=6? I_n = (n-1)(n-2)/2 = 5*4/2 = 10. So not all interior points are uncovered; only some. So complement size is 6. Then with k=3 sunny lines, we can cover at most 3 * L = 3 * 2 = 6 points from I_n (L=2). That's enough. So this configuration (x=1,y=1, hyp) gives complement size 6, and k=3 sunny lines can cover those 6 points. But we need to see if we can actually find 3 sunny lines covering those 6 points. The uncovered points are:

(2,2), (2,3), (2,4), (3,2), (3,3), (4,2).

We can try to pair them with sunny lines. For n=6, L = ⌊(n+1)/2⌋ - 1 = ⌊7/2⌋ - 1 = 3 - 1 = 2. So each sunny line can cover at most 2 points from this set. We need 3 sunny lines to cover 6 points, so each must cover exactly 2 points, and the lines must be disjoint in terms of covering these points (since a point cannot be covered by two lines? Actually it can be covered by multiple lines, but we need each point to be covered by at least one. The capacity bound is that with 3 sunny lines, the maximum number of distinct points they can cover from this set is 6, but they could also cover points outside, but we need to cover all. The question is: can we find 3 sunny lines (with slopes not 0,∞,-1) such that each covers two of these points, and together they cover all six? Let's examine possible pairs.

List points with coordinates:

A = (2,2)
B = (2,3)
C = (2,4)
D = (3,2)
E = (3,3)
F = (4,2)

Now, we need to see which pairs are collinear on a sunny line.

Compute slopes:

- A (2,2) and B (2,3): slope ∞ → vertical → not sunny.
- A and C (2,4): slope ∞ → not sunny.
- A and D (3,2): slope (2-2)/(3-2)=0 → horizontal → not sunny.
- A and E (3,3): slope (3-2)/(3-2)=1 → sunny.
- A and F (4,2): slope (2-2)/(4-2)=0 → horizontal → not sunny.

- B (2,3) and C (2,4): slope ∞ → not sunny.
- B and D (3,2): slope (2-3)/(3-2) = -1 → not sunny.
- B and E (3,3): slope (3-3)/(3-2)=0 → horizontal → not sunny.
- B and F (4,2): slope (2-3)/(4-2) = -1/2 → sunny.
- C (2,4) and D (3,2): slope (2-4)/(3-2) = -2 → sunny.
- C and E (3,3): slope (3-4)/(3-2) = -1 → not sunny.
- C and F (4,2): slope (2-4)/(4-2) = -2/2 = -1 → not sunny.
- D (3,2) and E (3,3): slope ∞ → not sunny.
- D and F (4,2): slope (2-2)/(4-3)=0 → horizontal → not sunny.
- E (3,3) and F (4,2): slope (2-3)/(4-3) = -1 → not sunny.

Thus, the only sunny pairs are:

- A–E (slope 1)
- B–F (slope –1/2)
- C–D (slope –2)

These are three disjoint pairs covering all six points. So we can take the three sunny lines:

L1: through (2,2) and (3,3) → y = x.
L2: through (2,3) and (4,2) → slope –1/2, equation y = –x/2 + 4? Compute: (2,3) → 3 = –1 + 4, ok.
L3: through (2,4) and (3,2) → slope –2, equation y = –2x + 8.

These lines are sunny. So indeed, for n=6, we have a covering with m=3 (x=1,y=1, hyp) and k=3 sunny lines. That's k=3, which is n-3? n=6 → n-3 = 3. So it's possible. So the maximum k for n=6 is at least 3. Could it be 4? That would be m=2. Let's test the bound from the interior points: For n=6, I_n size = 10. With m=2 non-sunny lines, the maximum number of I_n points they can cover is at most 2*(n-2)=2*4=8. The remaining I_n points must be covered by sunny lines. With k=4 sunny lines, each can cover at most L=2 I_n points, so they can cover at most 8. Thus total I_n coverage ≤ 8+8=16, which is >10, so not impossible. But we need to consider the structure of the non-sunny lines. For m=2, we can choose lines that cover many I_n points, but also need to cover axis points. However, the bound on I_n coverage from sunny lines is 2k, and from non-sunny lines is m*(n-2). So we have:

|I_n| ≤ m (n-2) + k L.

Plug numbers: m=2, n=6 → m(n-2)=2*4=8; k=4, L=2 → k L = 8; total 16 ≥ 10. So no contradiction. But we also need to cover axis points (E_n) of size 11. With m=2, we can cover axis points with non-sunny lines. The maximum axis points a non-sunny line can cover is n (if it's x=1 or y=1). But to cover 11 axis points with 2 non-sunny lines, we could use x=1 and y=1, which cover 9 axis points (since n=6 → 2n-1=11? Wait axis points for n=6 are 2n-1 = 11. x=1 covers (1,b) for b≤6 → 6 points; y=1 covers (a,1) for a≤6 → 6 points; overlap at (1,1) → union 11. So x=1,y=1 cover all axis points. That's m=2. So with m=2, we can cover all axis points. Then we have k=4 sunny lines to cover I_n points. As we saw, I_n size is 10. With 4 sunny lines, each can cover at most 2 I_n points, so they can cover up to 8 I_n points, but we need to cover 10 I_n points. Therefore, impossible. So for n=6, k cannot be 4. So the maximum k is 3. That matches n-3. For n=5, we had k=3 (n-2). So the pattern is not simply n-2 for all odd n; it's n-2 for n=3,5? For n=7, would k=5 be possible? That would be n-2=5. Let's check the bound: I_n size = (6*5)/2 = 15. With m=2 non-sunny lines, we can cover axis points. But the complement after m=2 is I_n (since x=1,y=1 cover axis). I_n size 15, with k=5 sunny lines, each can cover at most L = ⌊(n+1)/2⌋ - 1 = ⌊8/2⌋ - 1 = 4 - 1 = 3? Actually for n=7, n+1=8, L = ⌊8/2⌋ - 1 = 4 - 1 = 3. So 5 sunny lines can cover at most 5*3 = 15 I_n points. That's exactly the size. So it's possible in numbers. We need to see if we can actually find 5 sunny lines covering all I_n points. That's a covering problem. The I_n points correspond to P_6 after shifting? Actually I_n points for n=7 are those with a,b≥2, a+b≤8. That's a triangle of side 6 (since after shifting, a'=a-1,b'=b-1 ≥1, a'+b'≤6). So it's P_6. The maximum sunny points per line in P_6 is ⌊(6+1)/2⌋ = ⌊7/2⌋ = 3. So 5 lines can cover at most 15 points, which is the number of points. So if we can cover all points of P_6 with 5 sunny lines, then with m=2 non-sunny lines (x=1,y=1) we could cover the whole P_7. So the existence of such a covering would give k=5. That seems plausible. So we need a general result.

Thus, the problem reduces to: For a given n≥3, determine all k such that there exists a covering of P_n with n lines, exactly k of which are sunny. The construction for n=5 with k=3, n=6 with k=3, n=3 with k=1, n=4 with k=1, suggests that the maximum k is something like: if n is odd, max k = n-2; if n is even, max k = n-3? But we need to verify for n=7. Let's test quickly with a possible construction using m=2 (x=1,y=1) and k=5 sunny lines. The I_n points for n=7 are those with a,b≥2, a+b≤8. That's P_6 after shift. We need to cover P_6 with 5 sunny lines. Is that possible? Let's try to design. P_6 points (a,b) with a,b≥1, a+b≤7. That's 21 points. We already have a covering with 3 sunny lines and 2 non-sunny lines for n=6? Actually we just constructed for n=6 with m=3, k=3. That's different. For n=6, we had a covering with m=3,k=3. For n=7, we want m=2,k=5. That would be covering P_7. The complement after using x=1,y=1 is I_n of size 15. We need to cover those 15 points with 5 sunny lines. Each sunny line can cover at most 3 points from I_n. So we need a set of 5 sunny lines such that each point in I_n lies on at least one line, and no line has more than 3 points from I_n. This is a covering problem. We can try to extend the construction we used for n=5 and n=6. For n=5, I_n (size 6) was covered by 3 sunny lines each covering 2 points. For n=6, I_n (size 10) was covered by 3 sunny lines? Actually we used m=3, k=3 for n=6. That's not m=2. For m=2,k=4? That was impossible. But for m=2,k=5 (n=7), we need to cover I_n of size 15 with 5 sunny lines, each max 3 points. That's similar to covering a triangle of side 6 (P_6) with 5 lines of slopes not 0,∞,-1. That seems like a plausible covering. But is it always possible? We need to find a general construction for k = n-2 when n is odd, and k = n-3 when n is even? Let's test n=4 (even) → n-3=1, matches; n=6 (even) → n-3=3, matches; n=8 (even) → n-3=5. Would that be possible? We can try to see if there is a pattern: For even n, the maximum k seems to be n-3. For odd n, maximum k seems to be n-2. But we need to check n=3 (odd) → n-2=1, matches; n=5 (odd) → n-2=3, matches. So that's a hypothesis. Then the answer would be: all k from 0 to n-2 inclusive, except that when n is even, k cannot be n-2 (i.e., k = n-2 is impossible), but all smaller k are possible. However, we also need to check if k = n-3 is always possible for even n. For n=4, n-3=1 is possible. For n=6, n-3=3 is possible (we constructed). For n=8, we need to see if we can construct with m=5 (since k = n-3 = 5) and n=8, so m=3? Wait n=8, k=5 → m=3. That's not m=2. But we are looking for maximum k.
